BEHAVIORISTIC FOUNDATIONS OF UTILITY! 
By PATRICK SUPPES 
The central focus of this article is a derivation of stochastic utility functions 
from the basic postulates of stimulus sampling learning theory. Generalizations 
of the results to multiple choice situations are discussed, as well as relations to 
the entropy of a set of alternatives and to Luce’s choice axiom. 
IN THE past two decades there has been an intensive development of the 
subject of decision making. A variety of objectives and viewpoints has dom- 
inated the constructive as well as the critical work on the subject. Nonetheless 
a pervasive goal of nearly all contributors has been the elucidation of a 
theory of rationality for purposive behavior in situations of risk and un- 
certainty. Intuitively we expect every considered judgment or decision of a 
serious person to be rational in some definite sense. Certain authorities would 
maintain even that every considered decision of any mammalian organism 
is rational in the sense of representing the attempt to maximize some 
significant quantity. The most prominent “maximization” analysis of 
rationality is the thesis that the decision maker should maximize expected 
utility or value with respect to his beliefs concerning the facts of the situa- 
tion. To perform this maximization, he needs to have, or to act as if he had, 
a subjective probability function measuring his degrees of belief and a 
utility function measuring the relative value to him of the various possible 
outcomes of his actions or decisions. 
It is not my purpose here to expound the expected utility theory of 
behavior. An excellent detailed and leisurely analysis is Savage [13]. Rather 
my concern is to explore the extent to which behavioristic foundations can 
be supplied for utility. And I am using the term “‘behavioristic”’ in the rather 
narrow sense of the experimental psychologist. The static character of the 
concepts of subjective probability and utility is suspect to the psychologist 
and he resists accepting them as basic concepts of behavior. Ideally, what 
is desired is a dynamic theory of the inherent or environmental factors 
determining the acquisition of a particular set of beliefs or values. Moreover, 
in the notions of stimulus, response, and reinforcement the experimental 
psychologist has a triad of concepts which have proved adequate to explain 
much simple choice behavior. It is, therefore, a scientific problem of some 
1 This research was supported by the Rockefeller Foundation and the Group Psy- 
chology Branch of the Office of Naval Research. I have benefited from conversations 
with several people on the topic of this paper, but most particularly from those with 
Donald Davidson, William K. Estes, and Duncan Luce. Portions of this paper were 
presented at an International Colloquium on decision theory in Paris on May 27, 1959. 
186 


--- Page 79 ---

Av oe 
a oF ar OS =F 
ee we —~ 
n 
e 
BEHAVIORISTIC FOUNDATIONS OF UTILITY 187 
interest to try to use just these behavioristic notions to derive a theory of 
subjective probability and utility. 
In the first section I set forth the fundamental assumptions of stimulus 
sampling learning theory, which is the most formally sophisticated theory 
yet stated in terms of the concepts of stimulus, response, and reinforcement. 
In the second section I attempt to show how this theory may be used to 
derive a utility function for various simple choice situations. This derived 
utility function is for stochastic choice behavior of the kind studied by 
Davidson and Marschak [3], Luce [10], Papandreou [12], and others. In the 
third and final section the earlier results are related to Shannon’s concept of 
entropy and Luce’s choice axiom. 
1. STIMULUS SAMPLING LEARNING THEORY 
The theory to be used in this paper is a modification of stimulus sampling 
theory as first formulated by Estes and Burke [2, 8, 9]. It is most closely 
connected with a formulation given by Suppes and Atkinson [17], but it 
also differs, in ways indicated below, from the latter. The concepts of 
stimulus, response, and reinforcement and the processes of stimulus sampling 
and conditioning are the basic notions of the theory. In an economic situa- 
tion a typical stimulus might be the price set by a competitor for a given 
product during the past quarter; the response, the firm’s own price decision 
for the correct quarter; and the reinforcement or reward, the quarterly gross 
profits. In a simple two-choice experiment the single stimulus might be the 
light signaling onset of the trial; the response, the pressing of one of two keys 
used to predict which one of two lights will flash; and the reinforcement, 
the actual flashing of one of these two lights. 
The axioms are formulated verbally here, and although there is no 
attempt in this paper to give a mathematically exact statement of the 
theory, it is hoped that the relation between the fundamental axioms and 
the results derived later will be reasonably clear, even to the reader without 
prior familiarity with the literature. The first group of axioms deals with the 
conditioning of sampled stimuli, the second with the sampling of stimuli, 
and the third with responses. 
Conditioning Axioms: 
Cl. On every trial each stimulus element is conditioned to exactly one response. 
C2. If a stimulus element is sampled on a trial it becomes conditioned with 
probability 0 to the response (if any) which is reinforced on that trial. 
C3. If no reinforcement occurs on a trial there is a probability that the 
sampled stimulus becomes conditioned to some other response. 
C4. Stimulus elements which are not sampled on a given trial do not change 
their conditioning on that trial. 


--- Page 80 ---

188 PATRICK SUPPES 
C5. The probability of a sampled stimulus element being conditioned is 
independent of the trial number and the outcome of preceding trials. 
Sampling Axioms: 
Sl. Exactly one stimulus element is sampled on each trial. 
S2. If on a given trial it is known what stimuli are available for sampling, 
then no further knowledge of the subject’s past behavior or of the past pattern of 
reinforcement will change the probability of sampling a given element. 
Response Axiom: 
Rl. On any trial that response 1s made to which the sampled stimulus 
element is conditioned. 
Detailed remarks about these axioms are to be found in Suppes and 
Atkinson [17]. The major change from the version in [17] is to be found in 
Axiom C3. There this axiom reads: “If no reinforcement occurs on a trial 
there is no change in conditioning on that trial.’’ For the kind of experimental 
situation to be considered below it is natural to adopt the modified axiom 
given above as C3. A slight change in Axiom C5 has been made to accommo- 
date the major change in C3; otherwise the axioms given here are those of 
[17]. 
Many readers may be particularly critical of the first sampling axiom, S1. 
There are at least two different kinds of remarks to be made in defense of the 
assumption that exactly one stimulus element is sampled on each trial. In the 
first place, this assumption is mathematically extremely convenient and it is 
scarcely possible to distinguish, for the kind of experiments to be described 
here, between it and more “liberal” sampling axioms, as for example the 
assumption that all stimulus elements in the basic stimulus set are sampled 
with independent probabilities. Secondly, $1 may be made more intuitively 
plausible by interpreting ‘‘stimulus element’ to mean pattern of stimuli, for 
it may be maintained that in any given situation an organism, at any given 
moment, is sampling exactly one pattern of stimuli. (For a more detailed 
discussion of the pattern concept, see Estes [7].) 
We may consider two simple applications, which will be integrated into 
our discussion of utility in the next section. These two examples should 
serve adequately to illustrate how the basic axioms of stimulus sampling 
theory are related to particular experimental situations in order to make 
predictions about response behavior. 
Suppose the task presented a subject is tc predict on each trial exactly 
which one of two lights will come on. Thus on each trial exactly one of two 
reinforcing events, E; or Ez, occurs. The subject indicates his prediction at 
the beginning of each trial by pressing one of two keys, response A1 or Ag,

Rather than derive further predictions for the simple contingent case of 
reinforcement, I now turn to the second example, which I shall call the two-

the experimental situation to that of playing a slot machine with two arms 
or levers rather than one; on each trial a choice between the levers is made. ) 
(Mathematical statisticians have, during the past few years, considered in 
detail what is the optimal way to play a two-arm bandit for a finite number 
of trials when the probabilities of pay-off of the two arms are unknown.) 
The experimental situation, then, consists of choosing on each trial between

say, then either it pays off or it does not, without reference to the possible 
choice of lever 2. Such an analysis of reinforcement leads to an application 
of Axiom C3: if lever 7 is chosen (i.e., response A; occurs) and no reward or

2. UTILITY 
As indicated in the introductory section, in this paper I am mainly con- 
cerned with a utility function for the kind of choice behavior which has come 
to be labeled, not entirely happily, “‘stochastic.’’ Roughly speaking, the 
central character of stochastic choice behavior is that upon presentation of 
two alternatives a and 6, with a choice of one required, under essentially 
identical circumstances sometimes a will be chosen by a subject and some- 
times b. Let p (a, b), then, be the probability that a is chosen over b. A (stochastic) 
utility function for a set of alternatives A is a real-valued function u defined 
on A such that for every a, b,c and din A 
(4) p(a,b) >p(c,d) ifandonlyif u(a)—u(b) > u(c)—u(d). 
Combining results in Suppes and Winet [18], Suppes [16] and Davidson and 
Marschak [3], it may be shown that if the set A and the probabilities # (a, 5) 
satisfy the following axioms, then there exists a stochastic utility function 
for A, and moreover this function is unique up to a positive linear trans- 
formation. 


--- Page 86 ---

194 PATRICK SUPPES 
Axiom U1: (a,b) +p(b,a) = 1. 
Axiom U2: 0 < p(a,b) <1. 
Axiom U3: If p(a,b) > p(c,d) then p(a,c) > p(b,d). 
Axiom U4: There is acin A such that p(a,c) = p(c, bd). 
Axiom U5: If p(c,d) > p(a,b) > 1/2 then there is an e in A such that 
p(c,e) > 1/2 and p(e,d) > p(a,d). 
Axiom U6: (Archimedean Axiom). If p(a,b) > 1/2 then for every probability 
q such that p (a,b) >q > 1/2 there is a positive integer n such that q > p(a,ci) = 
p(c1,c2) = ... = p(en,b) > 1/2. 
Now one implication of these six axioms is that A must be an infinite 
set if for at least two members 4 and b of A, p(a,b) ~ 1/2. Simple and natural 
conditions, which are not unduly restricted and which will guarantee ex- 
istence of a stochastic utility function for a finite set A, are not easily found. 
An unworkable recursive, but not finite, axiomatization can be given by 
enumerating for each all isomorphism types. Some of the fundamental 
difficulties of finite axiomatization are brought out in Scott and Suppes [14]. 
The upshot of these axiomatic problems, it seems to me, is that for finite 
sets of alternatives we have no clear and intuitively natural ideas in terms 
only of probabilities of choice of the notion of utility, and thus of the notion 
of rationality for such situations.? 
On the other hand, we may apply the results of the preceding section to 
indicate how from the axioms of stimulus sampling theory a utility function 
may be derived for finite sets of alternatives. To begin with, let us consider 
the second example of the application of stimulus sampling theory, namely, 
the two-arm bandit. On each trial the subject must choose between two 
alternatives, but now, to make the utility considerations interesting, we 
assume there is a set of alternatives available, with choice restricted on each 
trial to one of a pair. Clearly alternative a does not in and of itself have more 
value than alternative 5; the value of a is determined by the probability of 
pay-off, as is that of b. Thus the experimenter may manipulate the value of 
any alternative according to his determination of its pay-off function. We

and the “‘technical axioms’ U1 and U2, it is obvious that the quadruple condition is 
also necessary and sufficient in this context. It may also be remarked that to give 
necessary and sufficient conditions on the set A and the function p, without continuity 
or finiteness restrictions, is the extremely difficult mathematical problem of classifying 
all isomorphism types representable by a real-valued function u satisfying (4).

196 PATRICK SUPPES 
It is straightforward to show that the utility function defined by (9) is 
unique up to a positive linear transformation if the reasonable restriction is 
made that any acceptable utility function must be continuous in ég and aq. 
Moreover, from the existence of a function u satisfying (4), it immediately 
follows that the asymptotic choice behavior predicted by stimulus sampling 
theory satisfies all the various conditions of weak and strong stochastic 
transitivity discussed in the literature, as well as the quadruple condition 
expressed by Axiom U3 above. It should be mentioned that these results do 
not necessarily hold during the course of learning; in particular the utility 
function defined by (9) does not satisfy (4) during the course of learning. 
This fact, it seems to me, accords well with the widespread assumption, 
albeit often tacit, that the utility function of a person isan equilibrium concept. 
It may also be noted that the numerical-valued utility function defined by 
(9) may be replaced by a function whose values are probability distributions 
if the basic theory is formulated so that p (a,b) is a random variable rather 
than a number. Unfortunately the derivation of the distribution of this 
random variable is tedious and difficult. As formulated here, the number 
p (a,b) is the asymptotic expectation of the response random variable that 
has the value | for choice of a and 0 for choice of b. The utility function of (9) 
is defined in terms of this expectation and is not sensitive to the trial-by- 
trial fluctuations in the values of the response random variable itself, which 
is another facet of its equilibrium character. 
It is, of course, to be emphasized that the utility function defined by (9) is 
not that of the mathematical statistician bent on maximizing his monetary 
pay-off in the long run. It should be abundantly clear that the whole theory 
of probabilistic choice behavior is not meant to apply to such a person. For 
under the pay-off conditions defined here, if 74 > » the statistician should 
have at asymptote ~(a,b) = 1. The point of (9) is rather to define a utility 
function which may be used to predict the actual behavior of all but the 
statistically sophisticated few. Numerous empirical studies (Mosteller and 
Nogee [11], Davidson, Suppes, and Siegel [4], Papandreou [12], Atkinson 
and Suppes [1], Davidson and Marschak [3]) have clearly shown that naive 
subjects do not behave like mathematical statisticians. Experimental data on 
utility functions as defined by (9) for the two-arm bandit situation will be 
reported elsewhere.

of simple contingent reinforcement discussed in the preceding section. By 
replacing zz by 1—ze2, for purposes of symmetry, thus having as reinforce-

get a utility function satisfying (4) by taking

Further remarks on this case do not seem necessary. 
The interesting question of generalization, it seems to me, is that of con- 
sidering situations in which choice is made from one of m alternatives. In 
classical economic theory, the resolution of this choice problem is immediate: 
simply choose the most preferred item. But, as far as I know, with the notable 
exception of Luce [10] there has been little if any analysis of stochastic 
choice behavior when the choice set has more than two alternatives. To 
describe this situation, let us use the notation ~ (a, A) to mean the probability 
a is chosen in preference to any member of A, with the understanding that 
{a}UA is the full choice set available, ie., p(a,A)+ p(A,a) = 1, where 
p(A,a) means the probability an element of A is chosen in preference to a.4 
Beginning simply with #(a,A), it is far from clear to me what axioms of 
rational behavior one might expect an organism to satisfy, in order to guaran- 
tee the existence of a utility function. In fact, it is not completely obvious 
what should be the defining characteristic of a utility function. In analogy to 
(4) I suggest: 
(10) p(a,A) > p(b,B) ifandonlyif u(a)—u(A) > u(b)—u(B). 
Condition (10) requires the utility of a set of alternatives to be defined, but 
it by no means implies that this set function need be additive, i.e., we need 
not have if A and B are disjoint sets that 
u(AUB) = u(A) + u(B). 
On the other hand, the intuitive interpretation of p(a, A) suggests that if A 
is a subset of B then the utility of A is equal to or less than B, for in some 
sense the utility of A is the overall value weighting assigned to the set in 
deciding to choose a rather than any member of A. Also, it seems reasonable 
to require that if the utility of A is equal to or greater than that of B anda 
set C is added to both A and B, with C disjoint from both A and B, then the 
utility of A UC is equal to or greater than that of BUC. These two principles 
may be summarized: 
(11) if ACB then u(A) <u(B), 
(12) if ANC = BNC = Oand u(A) < u(B) thenu(AUC) <u(BuUC). 
(Evidently (11) and (12) would not be acceptable if some of the alternatives 
had negative pay-offs, a possibility which we exclude here.) 
What I now want to show is that for this multi-choice case a utility 
function satisfying (10), (11), and (12) may be derived from the axioms of

sound. In particular, the marginal utility of adding another alternative to 
a set of such is appropriately a decreasing function of the size of the set. 
In other words, the utility function defined by (18) has the classical property 
that as wealth increases each additional unit has decreasing marginal utility.

Econometrica, Vol. 29, 2 (April 1961) 
AGGREGATION IN LEONTIEF MATRICES AND 
THE LABOUR THEORY OF VALUE 
By M. MorIsHIMA AND F. SETON 
This article examines the relationship between the price of commodities and 
ther total labour content when the productive contributions of all other fac- 
tors are imputed to labour. The transformation of one basis of valuation into 
the other, which entails the use of Leontief matrices, may be distorted by 
errors of aggregation. We examine the probable limits of these errors and the 
conditions for their total absence. 
SOVIET AND Eastern European economists have recently been troubled by a 
number of questions which might be brought under a common heading as 
the “inverse transformation problem.’’ After allowing an entirely artificial 
price system to emerge from uncoordinated ad hoc decrees, shifting fiscal and 
administrative requirements, or simple historical accident, they are increa- 
singly feeling the lack of rational economic criteria for investment choice, 
import policy, modernization measures, and similar decisions requiring some 
objective balancing of economic advantage against economic cost. In the 
search for such criteria their long-standing doctrinal preconceptions have 
naturally tended to drive them back to the Marxian value concept which, 
though constructed without regard for scarcity relations, does at least 
afford some objective (i.e., non-arbitrary) and consistent basis for attaching 
relative economic weights to the heterogeneous products which must enter 
their calculations. The initial problem to be faced has therefore been the 
conversion of prices into “values’’ (direct and indirect labour absorbed per 
unit of a commodity). 
The classical problem of the opposite conversion (values into prices) has 
exercised many minds since the appearance of the third volume of Das 
Kapital,! but the problem at issue here has so far only received attention 
as a by-product of the analysis of Leontief matrices. It has been shown,? 
for instance, that in a Leontief model the price of a commodity in terms of 
labour (“wage-price’’) will equal its Marxian “‘value’’ under certain conditions 
which include (i) competitive long-run equilibrium, i.e., zero profits in each 
sector, and (ii) perfect divisibility of the economy into “primitive sectors,’’ 
1 Béhm-Bawerk, Bortkievicz, Winternitz, Sweezy, May, Dobb, Meek, etc. (for exact 
references see Ronald Meek, ‘“‘Some Notes on the ‘Transformation Problem’,’’ Economic 
Journal, March, 1956, p. 94). See also F. Seton ,‘‘The Transformation Problem,” The 
Review of Economic Studies, June, 1957, pp. 149-160. 
2 N. Georgescu-Roegen, “‘Leontief’s System in the Light of Recent Results,” Review 
of Economics and Statistics, Vol. 32, 1950, p. 217, and B. Cameron, “The Labour 
Theory of Value in Leontief Models,’’ Economic Journal, Vol. LXII, 1952, pp. 191-7. 
203 


--- Page 96 ---

204 M. MORISHIMA AND F. SETON 
i.e., sectors producing single homogeneous commodities. Assumption (i) reduces 
the discovered equality to a merely formal one, since the destruction of the 
“surplus’’ (i.e., zero profit) does away with the substance of the Marxian 
analysis. 
It is our purpose in this article to inquire into the general relationship 
between Leontief price? and Marxian value when both assumptions (i) and 
(ii) are relaxed. In the first section we shall allow nonzero profits, but retain 
the postulate of perfect divisibility. This is the exact obverse of the model 
examined by one of the authors in a recent article. Cameron’s model5 will 
here emerge as a special case. 
In the second section we shall relax the postulate of perfect divisibility, 
and inquire into the possible distorting effects of the inevitable sector aggre- 
gations underlying all statistically knowable Leontief tables. 
Finally, in the third section, we shall apply the result of the general aggre- 
gation analysis to the special Marxian model characterized by the extreme 
form of aggregation into two departments only (capital goods and con- 
sumer goods).

The columns of (2) reproduce the familiar Marxian division of output into

It used to be held that the price-system was the only one which could 
be statistically known, and that the value-system was a metaphysical con- 
struct impervious to measurement or objective quantification of any sort. 
Our first task is to show that, given certain assumptions—all of them well 
grounded in the Marxian theory of value—this need not be so. Whatever the 
usefulness or irrelevance of the Marxian value concept as a description of 
“reality’’ or a guide to action, it is at least operationally meaningful.

pattern (workers in the linen industry may “‘subsist’’ mainly on potatoes; those in the 
jute industry mainly on rice—two wage goods which differ greatly in labour content). 
Insofar as the two assumptions forfeit their claim to reality they must be regarded as 
an alternative statement, rather than a justification, of postulate (5).

Further, since go—1 is the rate of profit o, and g9—1 is the rate of exploita- 
tion €9, this can be put in the form 
(9c) po = —— £0 
where x is the vector of cost of production c’w + v. 
By virtue of the fact that is positive and of unit sum, the fraction in (9c) 
represents the ratio of a weighted average of variable capitals (wage costs) 
to a similarly weighted average of total capitals (total costs), and is therefore 
obviously < 1. Accordingly equation (9c) allows us to draw two important 
conclusions: First, the rate of exploitation will always exceed the rate of 
profit ; and secondly, a Marxian type of ‘‘economic progress’’ (where capital 
accumulation steadily reduces the share of wages in the total costs of all 
sectors) will normally entail a fall in the rate of profit, unless accompanied 
by a countervailing increase in the rate of exploitation.14 
The formal correctness of these propositions does not, however, vindicate 
their political and ethical flavour, which is heavily dependent on the use of 
such emotive terms as “‘surplus’’ and “‘rate of exploitation.’’ More particu- 
larly, if the subsistence theory of wages is abandoned, workers might save, 
and equation (7) may cease to be true. At the same time the proposition that 
economic progress must entail a falling share of wages in total costs loses 
much of its former plausibility.15 Finally, a rising “‘rate of exploitation’ 
may come to mean no more than universally growing savings propensities in 
the wake of increased prosperity. 
14 It should be noted that equation (9c) differs from Mrs. Robinson’s interpretation 
of the Marxian profit-depressing mechanism (Joan Robinson, An Essay in Marxian 
Economics, p. 42). Her formula links the rate of exploitation with the rate of profit in 
value terms, say 1% = (vo/x%0)&0 which, as she acknowledges, is a mere tautology. But 
the money rate of profit po, as equation (9c) shows, is not strictly proportional to vo/xo, 
even if the rate of exploitation remains constant; for the modus operandi of “‘economic 
progress,’’ even if it reduced v/x by the same percentage in all sectors, could hardly 
fail to affect the matrix and therefore its latent vector u’ (see (9b)), thus altering the 
weights attaching to the various v/x. The decline in fo (with constant éo) is therefore 
no more than a strong presumption. 
15 While progress may well bring falling wage-shares in the costs of most industries, 
particularly manufacturing, experience suggests that this is normally counteracted by 
the growing relative importance of the most wage-intensive sectors, e.g., retail trading, 
passenger transport, services, etc. It is by no means certain, therefore, in which 
direction the ratio u’v/u’x in (9c) will move.

The transformation in a special model 
The general solution we have outlined ((8) and (9)), though necessary to 
prove the determinacy of the “‘inverse transformation problem,”’ issomewhat 
too complicated to serve as a basis for the aggregation problem which will 
be our main concern in the later sections of this article. It is therefore 
convenient to construct a simpler particular solution, by adding the following 
two assumptions: 
(10) Y= Vi 
and 
(11) Xidi= Uf. 
The first of these requires that the wage-bill paid out in any sector should be 
acceptable as a true measure of the (skill- and effort-weighted) labour power!6 
expended there, i.e., that labour should not earn quasi-rents in any sector 
of the economy. This would seem to be quite consistent with the competitive 
labour markets of classical models. The quantity of labour involved is here 
expressed in terms of one-£-earning time periods. 
The second assumption (11) is our interpretation of the Marxian dictum 
that “total price equals total value.’’ Though stated in the relevant passage 
of Das Kapital, this postulate is peripheral to Marxian value theory. It is 
implicitly contradicted by other statements in Marx’s work, many of which 
could equally well have been invoked in its stead. But its effect on the trans- 
formation is confined to the proportionality factor of the solution, and the 
algebraic convenience gained from it is certainly not bought at too damaging 
a price. 
Since (11) implies X(v-+ 0) = X(v+s), it follows from (5) and (10) that: 
4+s) (12) 00 = aid

ascertainable from the price system (1). By virtue of (10) and (12), equation 
(6) becomes 
(13) cw + rv = w

(22) XG, = X,. 
This is indeed no more than might have been expected. Since the “‘value’’ of 
the national income is by definition equal to the sum-total of labour expended 
it must remain unaffected by the way in which that labour is allocated 
among departments, and proposition (22) becomes self-evident. 
However, all the other elements of the interdepartmental value flow, along 
with their sums and sub-totals, will in general turn out to be different accord- 
ing to whether the “rough’’ or the “‘true’’ translation is used. 
“‘Non-distorting’’ aggregation 
A comparison of (20) and (21) may be expected to yield some information 
on the “distortions of aggregation’’ Q—Q. Where the aggregation is such 
that “rough’’ and “‘true’’ translations coincide (Q = Q) we shall speak of 
“non-distorting aggregation.”’ It is clear from (20) and (21) that a necessary 
and sufficient condition for such a situation may be stated as 
C'R = (A'cA’)’Q.

In words: An aggregation pattern of sectors into departments will be non- 
distorting if the resulting interdepartmental output quotas are the same in value 
terms as they are in price terms. 
In this general form the criterion is of little value as it presupposes a 
detailed knowledge of the original value system, which is precisely what the 
criterion should help us to dispense with. We can, however, improve matters 
by deriving two rather more stringent conditions from the general form: 
First, it is clear that (23) will be fulfilled, if 
D=4. 
In words: The aggregation will be non-distorting if the relative importance of the 
constituent sectors within each department 1s the same in value-terms as tt 1s in 
price-terms. The condition is of course more stringent than is necessary or 
desirable, but the knowledge of the original value system which it presupposes 
is only, as it were, one-dimensional, and might on occasion be supplied by 
intuition. 
Secondly, we can derive a condition for non-distorting aggregation that is 
independent of any knowledge of the value system at all: 
(24) C'A = Ac’. 
As post-multiplication by 4 shows, (24) implies C’AA = Ac’A. Since AA =I, 
it follows that C’ = J”, i.e., that the general condition (23) must be fulfilled. 
Condition (24) might be expressed as follows: Aggregation 1s non-distorting if 
only those sectors are aggregated into departments whose (departmental) output 
distributions follow the same pattern. 
A further sufficient condition, this time affecting input coefficients, may 
be derived by analogy with (24), and stated as

into value-terms 2 without any aggregation bias. 
Aggregation with limited distortions

equalities obtained as conditions for non-distorting aggregation are “‘relaxed”’ 
into inequalities specifying ranges rather than definite par values for the 
critical coefficients. We can then attempt to derive from these ranges certain 
consequential limits within which the distortions 2— 2 must lie.

A NOTE ON “AGGREGATION IN LEONTIEF MATRICES AND 
THE LABOUR THEORY OF VALUE” 
By Lr1r JOHANSEN 
THE INTENTION of this note is to add to Morishima’s and Seton’s paper! a 
corrollary regarding unit labour values; this requires some modification in 
the original notations used. 
Assume that there is defined a physical measure for the product of each 
sector and let the quantity produced by sector 7 be X;. Furthermore, let P; 
be the price per unit of product from sector 7 and Q; be the value per unit of 
product from sector 7. We then have

of the various sectors defined by the formula of Morishima and Seton depend

significance of this is that the values which can be computed by means of

Econometrica, Vol. 29, 2 (April 1961) 
NONLINEAR PROGRAMMING BY THE SIMPLEX METHOD 
By H. O. HartTLEy 
Nonlinear programming is a numerical technique of computing the 
“optimum levels”’ of “‘activities” for an organization or enterpreneur wishing 
to maximize an “objective function” (say, profit). While with ‘‘linear pro- 
gramming” profit is a “‘linear function” of the activity levels which are 
linearly constrained, the present methods permit nonlinear functional rela- 
tionships of a particular type. 
1. INTRODUCTION 
THE LITERATURE on mathematical programming concerned with nonlinear 
functionals has increased rapidly during the last decade. While numerous 
methods have been developed which are based on new algorithms specifically 
developed for particular situations, the scope of the simplex method for 
nonlinear programming problems has rightly received particular attention. 
Thus in the special case of ‘‘Quadratic Programming”’ we have witnessed both 
the development of new algorithms specifically designed to optimize a 
(semidefinite) quadratic form under linear constraints (see, e.g., Frank and 
Wolfe [5], Beale [1], Markowitz [7], and Houthakker [6}). At the same time 
the problem has been reduced to a “‘simplex-like’”’ procedure (Wolfe [10)). 
The present paper falls into the second category and is concerned with 
an extension of linear programming methods to nonlinear (not necessarily 
quadratic) functionals of a particular type. This restricted class of nonlinear 
functionals here treated (see Section 2) is closely related to the concept of 
“separable functionals’’ as defined by Charnes and Lemke [2]. These authors 
deal with an objective function composed of additive (nonlinear) components, 
each of which depends only on one of the activities x; (see our equation (1)). 
While Charnes and Lemke assume that the activities x; are linearly con- 
strained, we treat, in this paper, a more general form of nonlinear restric- 
tions also of a “‘separable’’ nature (see our inequalities (6)). As in their work, 
we require here an assumption of convexity for certain functionals and we 
also employ (as they did) polygonal approximations to these functions. A 
similar method of polygonal approximation has recently been used by 
G. Dantzig, S. Johnson, and W. White [3] in an elegant solution of the prob- 
lem of chemical equilibrium. 
We are able to reduce the solution of this (separable) type of nonlinear 
programming problem to a comparatively simple “‘simplex-like’’ algorithm. 
It is not claimed there may not be alternative procedures for solving this 
problem ; in fact it has been brought to our notice that the problem can be 
treated as a special case of the “‘Decomposition Principle’ recently developed 
by Dantzig and Wolfe [3]. Moreover, an alternative computational approach 
223 


--- Page 116 ---

224 H. O. HARTLEY 
may well employ the algorithm associated with bounded variables in the 
dual problem (see, e.g., Wagner [9}). 
It will be apparent, however, from Section 5 that the present approach 
leads to an algorithm which is so closely akin to the modified simplex method 
that computer codes for the latter may, with little modification, be used for 
the solution of the present nonlinear problems in mathematical programming. 
Moreover, it is shown in Section 6 that our method can be generalized to 
cover a certain class of nonseparable objective functions including quadratic 
ones. Thus quadratic programming under separable constraints (not neces- 
sarily linear constraints) is covered as a special case by the algorithm here 
developed. In the special case of quadratic programming under linear con- 
straints the efficient specialized algorithms developed in the papers mentioned 
above are no doubt preferable to the present ‘‘package deal’’ approach.

that more additional resources are required to earn an additional dollar 
when the level of activity is already high and less resources are required to 
earn that dollar when the level of activity is low.

6. GENERALIZATION TO NONSEPARABLE OBJECTIVE FUNCTIONS 
So far we have considered a form of the objective function (1) which is 
a sum of additive contributions + g;(x;) each contributed by a single 
activity. That is, different activities x; are interdependent only through 
their competing for the available resources c; in the » constraints. Apart 
from this competition for resources, however, each individual activity con- 
tributes a profit +g;(x;) which is completely independent of the levels x; 
with which the other activities occur in the enterprise (firm) considered. 
Such conditions are, of course, only satisfied if the level of production of x; 
by the enterprise does in no way effect the profit (market) for the activity %;. 
While such assumptions are fairly well satisfied for a comparatively non- 
monopolistic enterprise such as a farm, this may not be so for large monopo- 
listic ones. Of course, if the various activity outputs x of the firm enter 
completely independent markets, our assumptions would still be fairly 
well satisfied, in spite of the fact that some of the x; may be monopolistic. 
It has long been considered necessary, however, to study situations in which 
the profit rate from one activity depends on the levels at which the other 
activities are executed. The simplest mathematical model of such “price 
interdependence’”’ or “‘interaction’’ between activities is afforded by a quad- 
ratic objective function 
(38) Q ra Di aij%4X; + Dduxy = x'Ax+dx, 
aj i 
and this form of the objective function (in which the quadratic form is 
usually assumed to be negative semidefinite) has received considerable 
attention in the case in which the activities are linearly constrained (see 
particularly Wolfe [10], and also Markovitz [7], Houthakker [6], and Beale 
[1]). We shall now show that the algorithm of Section 5 can deal with the

which contradicts the fact that P, is the maximum for the vth simplex problem. It is 
clear that no statement can be made about the convergence to the solution vector 
(y*) of the vector (,y)(say) which yields the maximum profit P, for the vth simplex 
problem. Indeed such a convergence cannot be expected when the nonlinear problem 
does not have a unique solution (it may be a linear degenerate problem). If the non- 
linear problem has a unique solution, then the convergence of (,y) to (y*) can clearly 
be inferred because of (11). It is conceivable that by a standardization of tie breaking 
in the simplex procedures convergence can be assured in the degenerate case but we 
shall not enter into this problem here.

A NOTE ON THE RESIDUAL VARIANCE ESTIMATION 
IN SIMULTANEOUS EQUATIONS! 
By A. L. NAGAR 
We consider in this note the bias of the residual variance estimator in 
simultaneous equations according to the k-class method. An unbiased esti- 
mator is formulated. 
1. INTRODUCTION 
THIS NOTE is the sequel to my paper [1]; it deals with the estimation of the 
residual variance of a stochastic equation which is part of a system of 
simultaneous equations. The estimation procedure followed is that of the 
k-class. The bias (to the order of 1/T, T being the number of observations) 
of the residual variance estimator is derived and an unbiased estimator (to 
the order 1/T) is formulated.

Econometrica, Vol. 29, 2 (April 1961) 
A NOTE ON THE GENERAL POSSIBILITY THEOREM OF 
THE SOCIAL WELFARE FUNCTION! 
By YASUSUKE MURAKAMI 
K. J. Arrow’s general possibility theorem is justly criticized and revised by 
J. H. Blau. But Blau’s revised theorem is based on by far stronger assump- 
tions than Arrow’s, and, therefore, seems to lose a great deal of its practical 
significance. This paper aims to show that Blau’s criticism is not so serious, | 
as it appears and that the essence of Arrow’s argument remains virtually 
unchanged and equally challenging to the interested social scientist. | 
In HIs paper [1] Blau proves that the general possibility theorem of the 
social welfare function does hold if all conceivably possible combinations of 
individual orderings are admitted—or, in his terminology, if all triples of 
alternatives are free. In Arrow’s original version [2] of the theorem which 
was proved by Blau to be generally not true, the condition necessary for the 
theorem is merely that at least one triple be free. There is a vast difference 
between these two conditions. 
Suppose that we face an infinite number of alternatives. In such a case, 
it is highly probable that at least one triple among those infinitely many is 
free. But almost every individual ordering is restricted in some way or other, 
and it is hardly probable that all triples are free. Thus, it seems as if Blau’s 
revision vastly weakened or almost nullified the practical applicability of ) 
the theorem. 
The purpose of this note is to show that Blau’s criticism is, in fact, not a 
serious one—far less serious than it appears in Blau’s paper. For that 
reason, I shall try to present conditions for Arrow’s result which are more 
plausible and yet not less appropriate than Blau’s. For convenience’s sake, 
I shall follow Blau’s well-phrased terminology and notation. Blau’s main 
theorem is: ) 
THEOREM (Blau): If the number of alternatives is at least three, and if all 
triples are free, then no social welfare function can satisfy conditions 2’, 3, 5, 
and the URP (the unanimity rule of preference). 
In Blau’s expression, Arrow’s original version, which was proved to be 
generally not true, is: 
1 The writer should like to express his gratitude to Professor K. J. Arrow of Stanford 
University, Mr. Ken-ichi Inada of Tokyo Metropolitan University, and Mr. Tadashi 
Hayasaka of Tokyo University for their valuable advice and comments. But only the 
writer is responsible for possible mistakes. 
244 


--- Page 137 ---

oe we ee ee 
n 
if 
oo CDi ae ee 
> 
rd 
hi 
he 
GENERAL POSSIBILITY THEOREM 245 
THEOREM (Arrow): If there exists at least one free triple, then no social welfare 
function can satisfy conditions 2, 3, 4, and 5. 
Observe here that conditons 2’, 3, 4, and the URP are hereditary (or, if 
those conditions hold on a set . of alternatives, then they do so on each 
subset of .o7). And also observe that conditions 2 and 5 are not hereditary. The 
following lemma is self-evident. 
Lemma: If all relevant conditions hold on some subset of , and if the number 
of alternatives of that subset 1s not less than three, then Blau’s theorem on a set 
& does hold as well on that subset. 
Thus, if condition 5 is assumed to hold on some particular subset of alterna- 
tives, Blau’s theorem can hold on that particular subset. We introduce here 
the following definition. 
DEFINITION: If there is an individual all of whose preferences on some subset 
of alternatives are adopted always by society, then he is said to be a dictator on 
that subset. 
Then, the following theorem can be established from Blau’s theorem and our 
lemma. 
THEOREM |: If there exists some subset of alternatives on which no individual 
is a dictator and all triples are free, then no social welfare function can satisfy 
conditions 2’, 3, (5),2 and the URP. 
Evidently, Blau’s theorem is a special case of this theorem. We have now 
had the more general version of the general possibility theorem. 
As another special case of Theorem | which is antipodal to Blau’s theorem, 
we can establish the following proposition: 
THEOREM 2: If there exists one free triple on which no individual ts a dictator 
then no social welfare function can satisfy conditions 2’, 3, (5),3 and the URP. 
It can be easily proved that, in any free triple and under conditions 2’ and 
3, the condition URP is equivalent to condition 4. Therefore, we have: 
THEOREM 3: If there exists one free triple on which no individual is a dictator, 
then no social welfare function can satisfy conditions 2’, 3, 4, (and 5).4 
2 Condition 5 is in parentheses because it is automatically derived from the non- 
dictatorial condition concerning the free subset and is, in fact, unnecessary. 
3 [bid. 
4 Ibid. 


--- Page 138 ---

246 YASUSUKE MURAKAMI 
The last form of the theorem becomes again similar to Arrow’s original 
version. But we can find changes in the replacement of condition 2 by 2’ as 
well as the revision of the condition that one free triple exists. Condition 2’ 
is essential for the proof of the theorem. 
The secret of Blau’s successful criticism lies in his full utilization of a 
peculiar property of Arrow’s nondictatorship condition. As is seen in our 
Theorem 1, the general possibility theorem can hold only with the concurrence 
of nondictatorship and freedom of individual orderings in some subset of 
alternatives. It is because Arrow’s nondictatorship condition is very weak 
and nondictatorship is not definitely located that Blau has to widen the 
range of freedom and therefore to strengthen the freedom condition. But the 
definition of dictatorship in this condition 5 can be said to be too strong in 
the sense that, even if some individual can impose his preference concerning 
all pairs of alternatives except for only one pair, he can still be nondictatorial. 
If we consider the usual political-economic implication of dictatorship, 
we may well be justified in weakening the definition and comprehending the 
wider variety of situations. Thus, we have weakened the nondictatorship 
condition in the above theorems. 
The theorems which we have already shown are, in a sense, corollaries of 
Blau’s theorem. But the meaning of this derivation is not trivial. For we 
have been able to reconvert the theorem into the form of the existence of 
one free triple with a certain property. The one-free-triple-existence con- 
dition proposed above is only a slight and justifiable modification of Arrow’s 
old one. We may well conclude that the practical significance of Arrow’s 
proposition is hardly injured in spite of Blau’s ingenious argument. 
Tokyo University

No. 2, April, 1957.

ON THE EXISTENCE OF GENERAL EQUILIBRIUM: 
SOME CORRECTIONS 
By LIoNEL W. McKENZIE 
A CAREFUL audit of the argument of my article “On the Existence of 
General Equilibrium for a Competitive Market’’ [2] by Gerard Debreu has 
exposed a weakness in the definition of irreducible markets which should be 
corrected. As the definition now stands, the extension of the results to non- 
linear models in Theorem 3 cannot be made. The necessary amendment, 
however, is not difficult. Also, for the purpose of this Theorem, Assumption 5 
needs a small revision. 
The definition of irreducible markets as it now stands is contained in 
Assumption 6, [2, p. 58]: 
“However I; and Iz may be selected, if x1, lies in Y— X7,, then there 
is also we Y — X;, such that w = x7,— x1, and xj (=) x; for all teJ; and 
xi (>) x for some te];.”’ 
The difficulty with this definition is that the J; consumers will be unable 
to take w if it contains a resource whose supply they cannot increase above 
what is offered in x;,. On the convention which is used in Section 7, in 
approaching Theorem 3, this difficulty always arises with respect to the J; 
entrepreneurial resources. Thus these resources must not appear in w, and 
Y is effectively reduced to production which employs only Jz entrepreneurial 
resources.! The restriction could be recognized by applying Assumption 6 
to the extended model directly, replacing the variables with their capped 
analogues. Such a restriction is, however, unnecessary. Indeed, a difficulty 
can easily arise when no entrepreneurial resources are present, with land, 
for example, when none of it is withheld for direct use by the consumers. 
The preferable solution is to revise Assumption 6. It may be rephrased as 
follows: 
“However I; and I, may be selected, if xz, = y— xz, with yeY and 
x1,€Xy7,, then there is also y’eY and weX,,, such that x7, = y’— x7,—w 
and xi (=) x; for all ¢eZ2, and xj (>) x for some ¢eJ;.”’ 
This revision actually corresponds better with the verbal discussion of 
the Assumption on p. 59 [2] than the present version. Now the Assumption 
implies its capped analogue and the extension of Section 7 to the model with 
firms can go forward. It should be noted, however, that w and x7, may swell 
the quantity of Jz resources beyond what is useful for J; consumers. Then the 
surplus must be disposed of in some way. This is no bother with the entre- 
preneurial resources, since they are freely disposed of in the extended model. 
1 This was observed by Debreu. I am very grateful to him for the care he has taken 
to examine my arguments. 
247 


--- Page 140 ---

248 LIONEL W. MCKENZIE 
Neither the old nor the new version of Assumption 6 implies the other. 
Thus an even weaker assumption would present the two versions as alterna- 
tives. The gain from preserving the old version in the new, however, seems 
too slight to justify the complication. 
The change in the statement of Assumption 6 requires a corresponding 
modification of the proof of Theorem 2 on p. 66. The new argument is 
simpler. From the middle of line 6, it should now read. 
“Then by Assumption 6 there is ye Y and wex 1, such that w = y— x1,— XI,» 
where x (=) x4 for all tel, and xi, (>) Xi, for some t¢1;. Since x4, is pre- 
ferred, it cannot be available, Or Pp" "> 0. On the other hand, by Assump- 
tion 6 the 7th consumer is not satiated at x*eX  Y, nor, therefore, at p*, 
for any teJ;. Then Lemma 2 implies p* - x; = 0 for any x; Ce (p*) for tel. 
Thus p* - %;, >0. But £*-y < 0, because p*eP, and p*- Xi, = 0 by con- 
tinuity. Therefore, p* - wi < 0 for some teI2, which contradicts the defini- 
tion of Ig. So Iz is empty.”’ 
The other revision which Section 7 demands is also a consequence of the 
presence of entrepreneurial resources in the extended model. Either we must 
apply Assumption 5 directly to the extended model, or we must revise the 
first part of the assumption. The first part now reads: 
“Aen yY £%." 
The analogue version of this is not implied, however, since the 7th consumer 
lacks some of the entrepreneurial resources.2 We must either apply the 
assumption to the analogue model directly, or change the assumption. The 
former is more general, but the gain is slight over assuming 
Ota. 
This says that each consumer can survive without trade. It is one of the 
assumptions of Arrow-Debreu [1].° 
With these corrections Theorem 3 may be established. Theorem 2 of 
Arrow-Debreu is a special case of Theorem 3. Finally, part (c) of Assumption 
6* [2, p. 71] needs a revision parallel to the revision I have described for 
Assumption 6.

LETTER TO THE EDITOR 
DEAR SIR: 
We have been asked on several occasions whether the GCL or ‘two-stage least- 
squares’’! estimates, Bni,fax, of structural coefficients in systems like 
(1) By! + I'x, +e) =(Q, 
(2) Y,, = Hx, +0, 
could be improved in some sense by an iteration of the two-stage process. That is, 
Fs a column vector of calculated values derived from direct estimates of (2), is 
replaced in (1) by 9}, a second column vector of calculated values derived from 
(3) ¥en es, 
to obtain iterated estimates B, I’; ¥,, is replaced in turn by a third vector of 
calculated values, etc. 
This question merits consideration, but the answer is in the negative, for the 
estimates Bre and }nx obtained by the iteration will be identical with the GCL 
estimates Bui and Yak. 
In the proof that follows, the notation employed, the dimensions of matrices, 
and the partition of JJ will be identical with that employed on pp. 100-102 in the 
paper by R. L. Basmann, ‘‘On the Asymptotic Distribution of Generalized Linear 
Estimators,’ Econometrica, Vol. 28, 1, January, 1960.

Econometrica, 29, Vol. 2 (April 1961) 
BOOK REVIEWS 
Ekonomicheski raschet nailuchshego ispol’ zovania resursov (Economic Calcula- 
tion of the Best Utilization of Resources), By L. V. KANtTorovicH. Moscow: 
Academy of Sciences of the U.S.S.R. 1959. 
BEFORE REVIEWING Kantorovich’s new book, it may be appropiate to retrace his 
long-neglected historical contribution to the development of linear programming. 
In 1938-1939, L. V. Kantorovich, a Leningrad mathematician, worked in 
consultation with a research laboratory of the plywood industry on the problem 
of allocating a number of different machines among products requiring various 
amounts of machine-time so as to maximize the output of the products in certain 
desired proportions. In July 1939, he published his results in a pamphlet issued 
by the Leningrad State University1. In addition to his detailed treatment of the 
machine problem, he sketched out methods for selecting an optimum crop- 
rotation scheme, for the rational routing of transported goods, and for minimizing 
the waste of materials cut from standard forms. These were essentially linear 
programming problems, one of them—the metal-trimming problem—being of 
the most general sort that can be shown to be equivalent to a matrix-game 
problem2. 
As in the case of George B. Dantzig’s simplex method, which was developed 
in 1947-1948 independently of the earlier Russian work, Kantorovich’s method 
of solution made use of implicit prices (‘resolving multipliers’ in his terminology) 
to improve allocation, prices and activity levels being successively adjusted until 
an optimal solution was achieved. However, since the algorithm was not fully 
described in the 1939 paper, it is not clear whether the method would necessarily 
always converge toward an optimum in a finite number of steps. In an appendix, 
Kantorovich, anticipating the later work of Koopmans and others, used geomet- 
ric methods to prove the existence of the ‘‘multipliers” for the machine-assign- 
ment problems he was investigating. He identified these multipliers with the 
direction coefficients of the supporting plane to the convex polyhedron represent- 
ing the attainable commodity bundles at a point corresponding to the desired ) 
proportions of output. 
Later articles on the transportation problem (1942 and 1949) and a number ? 
of mathematical papers on the formal aspects of what has become known as 
activity analysis round out Kantorovich’s contributions to the field, up to the 
publication of the book under review. 
1 L. V. Kantorovich, Matematicheskie metody organizatsii 1 planivovania proizvodstva 
(Mathematical Methods of Organizing and Planning Production), Leningrad State 
University, 1939. 
2 For details, see T. C. Koopmans’s introductory remarks to the translation of 
Kantorovich’s paper ‘‘Mathematical Methods of Organizing and Planning Production,” 
Management Science, July 1960. 
8 Activity Analysis of Production and Allocation (Tjalling C. Koopmans, ed.). John 
Wiley and Sons, Inc., New York, 1951, chs. 3, 17, 18, and 19. 
252 


--- Page 145 ---

1a 
te 
of 
” 
in 
<< 
BOOK REVIEWS 253 
This recent book is far wider in scope than any of the author’s previous publica- 
tions. While it contains a number of examples drawn from the practice of indivi- 
dual enterprises, it also stresses economy-wide problems and the general principles 
of efficient allocation: at a point of time (static model), through space (trans- 
portation problem), and through time (dynamic model). He lays particular emphasis 
on the use of efficient prices, derived from the solution of a linear program, to bring 
about marginal improvements in resource allocation, without the need to resort 
to a recomputation of the entire program. He claims that the implicit prices are 
sufficiently stable—given small changes in the demand for the output and in the 
set of attainable points—to justify this sort of approximation (p. 52). Since he 
assigns implicit prices (‘‘objectively determined valuations’ in his new termino- 
logy) to all scarce resources, including the rental services of capital goods, better 
types of land, transportation capacity and labor of different skills, the price 
system he advocates would differ markedly from the producers’ prices actually in 
force in the Soviet Union, which are built up from direct and indirect labor costs 
plus a small, arbitrarily set, profit margin. (Although Kantorovich stresses time 
and again that labor is the source of all value, it is clear nevertheless that he 
interprets this notion more flexibly than most Soviet economists.) He does not 
hesitate to call for a reform of Soviet prices, which would take into account the 
scarcity of factors other than labor. Such a reform, he predicts, would tend to 
raise the relative prices of rolled steel, oil, coal, cement, and railroad transporta- 
tion services (p. 155). 
In his chapter on dynamic programming, which is geared to the problem of 
investment choice, Kantorovich presses for the adoption of a discount rate, 
appropriate for use in the entire economy, but which would of course have to 
correspond to costs and returns calculated at efficient prices. His criticism of 
the rules-of-thumb employed at present by Soviet ‘‘project-makers’’ for choosing 
among investment alternatives is trenchant. 
Kantorovich’s interest in efficient prices as a guide to rational allocation has 
apparently led him to the independent discovery of the basic properties of such 
prices and of the costs on which they might be based. On pages 82-84, he reasons 
out that the value of the incremental product of a factor should be equal in 
different uses; on page 164, he states the principle that it is rational for an 
enterprise to increase its scale of output up to the point where the (efficient) price 
of each product just covers its incremental cost; and finally, on pages 215-217, 
he develops the notion of consumers’ surplus in connection with an investment in 
new railroad capacity, where he assumes what amounts to a sloping demand curve 
for the transportation service. (He criticizes the capitalist system for its failure 
to make this type of project appear profitable by market criteria.) 
Kantorovich does not believe in the institution of a free market for producer 
goods, from which efficient prices would emerge as the resultant of supply and 
demand forces, but in using industry-wide and eventually economy-wide linear 
programs to compute these efficient prices—thus circumventing the need for 
decentralizing the entire economy. He seems to realize the difficulties of collecting 
the data for such a task and of calculating a solution, even with the most up-to- 
date electronic machinery. But he is convinced that even approximations and 


--- Page 146 ---

254 BOOK REVIEWS 
partial solutions (limited to an industry or a single region) would be better than 
the system as it operates at present. In his opinion, the ‘‘net output” of the 
economy would stand to be increased by 30 to 50 percent if the linear program- 
ming methods he advocates were adopted (p. 17). 
The author relegates the mathematics of linear programming to two long 
appendices, one on concepts and the other on computing techniques, which revise 
and somewhat amplify his earlier studies. Both appendices are well suited to 
formalize the more general discussion in the text, and they should be of great 
help to Soviet scholars wishing to master the new techniques. The appendices, 
however, cover relatively little theoretical matter not already available in the 
West. The novel aspect, from the viewpoint of Western specialists, is the postula- 
tion for every concrete problem of an inflexible product-mix (instead of the more 
familiar objective function, linear or concave). This assumption somewhat com- 
plicates the exposition but it is appropriate under Soviet conditions, since the 
choice of final products involves delicate problems of consumer sovereignty as 
well as other issues concerning the development of the economy which are the 
exclusive province of political decisions. The appendix on computational methods 
sets forth the author’s version of the revised simplex method as well as a variant 
of the 1939 algorithm, which, Kantorovich states, is closely related to the work 
of Dantzig, Ford, and Fulkerson‘. 
The significance of this work lies in several directions. It is the first Soviet 
textbook on linear programming, written by a scholar who made a basic contribu- 
tion to the field. It is also the first major Soviet work in perhaps thirty years 
which deals in a systematic way with the general problem of the optimum use 
of scarce resources. Finally, it lays a bridge between Soviet and Western economic 
thought. Considering all these innovations, we should not be surprised to hear 
that Kantorovich has had to overcome a good deal of inertia and opposition to 
his ideas. Academician Nemchinov, who wrote the preface to the book, was 
careful to dissociate himself from the author’s treatment of economy-wide prob- 
lems and from his criticisms of the Soviet price-system. In Nemchinov’s view, 
the new methods may be applicable to solve problems faced by single enterprises, 
perhaps even by an entire industry, but he argues that Kantorovich’s ‘‘preten- 
sions’ regarding their wider employment (e.g., in framing national plans) lack all 
foundation. Kantorovich has also been criticized for his marginalist conceptions 
and for his neglect of Marxist methodology (Vestntk statistiki, No. 9, 1959, pp. 
61-62, and Planovoe khoziaistvo, No. 1, 1960, pp. 92-96). 
In view of the importance of this book to many groups of economists—-including 
specialists in the Soviet field, in management science, and in programming techni- 
ques—it is to be hoped that it will be translated at the earliest opportunity. 
Yale University J. M. Montias

BOOK REVIEWS 255 
Econometrics. By STEFAN VALAVANIS. Edited, from manuscript, by ALFRED 
H. ConrapD. Economics Handbook Series, edited by SEyMour E. Harris. New 
York: McGraw-Hill Book Company, Inc. 1959. xvii + 223 pp. $ 7.00. 
PUBLISHED POSTHUMOUSLY, this introductory text reaffirms the tragedy of the 
premature death of Valavanis!. Probably not since Frank Ramsey have the social 
sciences lost a youthful scholar of such proven promise and, as this book proves, 
of supreme pedagogical skill. It would be difficult to overpraise the author’s 
exposition. Here we have a text that not only explains lucidly, but one that 
interests and stimulates too. Reflecting the author’s sparkling imagination, the 
book abounds in surprises and amusements for the econometric expert. 
Throughout, although emphasis is upon research strategy, the book is non- 
empirical. Nor does it endeavour to provide a systematic outline of econometrics. 
Its ambit is to introduce maximum likelihood methods. In doing so, Valavanis 
proves his hypothesis that it is possible to reduce to common-sense understanding 
the mathematical statistics underlying econometric theory. Still, some mathema- 
tical background—elementary statistical concepts, algebra, a little calculus— is 
expected of the reader. 
The author has organised his material into 12 chapters, each with a carefully 
oriented list of further readings and a number of exercises that, in the main, 
extend the text. Scattered throughout are a number of short digressions on ancil- 
lary topics, for example, Jacobians and directional least squares, felt to be in 
need of a simple explanation. A number of more diversionary topics, such as 
computational layout and a few mathematical proofs, are relegated to appendices. 
Chapter 1 introduces such fundamentals as the distinctions between universe, 
population, and sample; parameter and estimate; and the role of the stochastic 
error term and its associated assumptions relevant to maximum likelihood. 
Chapters 2 and 3 cover single equation models. Outlined are the mechanics of 
maximum likelihood and its relation to least squares, the concepts of bias and 
consistency, and the implications of lags. 
The pitfalls and problems of estimation relative to many-equation linear models 
are expounded in Chapters 4 to 6. Here we find an excellent pedagogic treatment 
running through the treachery of naive least squares for simultaneous systems, 
the identification problem, and a much needed exposition of the relationship 
between underidentification, multicollinearity, and confluence. Chapters 7, 8, 
and 9 consider, respectively, the estimating techniques of instrumental variables, 
limited information and the method of dual reduced forms. Although the latter 
approach was developed independently by Theil? and Basmann, no reference is 
made to Basmann. This is somewhat inexplicable, given the abundance of mis- 
1 It should be recorded that in his early work, Valavanis wrote under the name of 
Stefan Vail. 
2 H. Theil, ‘Estimation of Parameters of Econometric Models,”’ Bulletin de l’institui 
international de statistique, Vol. 34, pt. 2 (1954), pp. 122-129. 
3 R. L. Basmann, ‘‘A Generalized Classical Method of Linear Estimation of Coeffi- 
cients in a Structural Equation,” Econometrics, Vol. 25 (1957), pp. 77-84. 


--- Page 148 ---

256 BOOK REVIEWS 
prints in the Theil reference. The implications of overidentification and under- 
identification for research strategy are also discussed in Chapter 9. 
The final three chapters appear somewhat disjointed. Still, this is probably 
more than balanced by the fact that they treat topics of importance whose in- 
clusion earlier would have been rather diversionary. The searching for and testing 
of hypotheses is discussed in Chapter 10. Here, inter alia, is a critical exposition 
of bunch map analysis—a tool that seems to hold perennial attraction and con- 
fusions for incipient econometricians. The estimation of unspecified factor effects, 
such as technological change and degree of monopoly, is briefly discussed in 
Chapter 11, primary emphasis being given to linear orthogonal factor analysis. 
Chapter 12 introduces time series, a subject unworthy of separate treatment ‘‘were 
it not for the muddle and confusion we have inherited”’ (p. 166). The inadequacies 
of the National Bureau’s and other more naive approaches to business cycle 
analysis are discussed forcefully. Correlograms and periodograms, the variate 
difference method, and the relation between cross section and time series estimates 
are also critically scrutinized. 
However, despite the pedagogic merits of the arrangement of material in the 
book, there are quite a few errors in the material presented—perhaps indicating 
the manuscript was not in final form. To give a few examples: the sample moment 
for a particular sample is defined in expected terms (p. 20); S rather than S—1 
is used in referring to the variance estimated from a sample (p. 51) ; the disturbance 
assumptions for maximum likelihood estimation are incomplete, independence 
between all disturbances being necessary, not merely the lack of autocorrelation 
(p. 77); and exercises 3.D, 5.A and 5.B are misleading. Accordingly, the book can 
hardly be recommended for beginning students unless supplemented by a list of 
corrigenda, although its pedagogic innovations should be suggestive to teachers in 
arranging their mode of presentation. 
University of Adelaide Joun L. Ditton 
Methods of Correlation and Regression Analysis, Linear and Curvilinear. By 
MorvECAI EZEKIEL AND Kart A. Fox. New York: John Wiley and Sons. 3rd 
edition. 1959. vii +- 548 pp. $10.95. 
IN ITs two first editions of 1930 and 1941, Ezekiel’s treatise has been a basic text 
in statistical courses in the socioeconomic sciences. The persistent demand for 
the book is easy to understand in view of its emphasis on (1) causal analysis of 
non-experimental data, and (2) elementary and common sense approaches, and 
in particular on the method of least squares regression. If the book is still rather 
alone with such a program, this reflects that applied statistics for a long time has 
been in a stage of expansion and gradual transition. On the one side we have the 
forceful development of experimental statistics; on the other we note the new 
development of interdependent systems in econometrics with their wholesale 
dismissal of single-relation approaches, and in particular of least squares regres- 
sion. 
The reverse side of the coin is that the treatise leaves much to be desired with 


--- Page 149 ---

~~ ~~ — VS 
~~ — DO DO — et VE OD a= See ae | ee 
~~ 
BOOK REVIEWS 257 
regard to mathematical rigour. As to least squares regression, the method is not 
provided with a clearcut rationale; rather it emerges as a rule of thumb that by 
experience works well in practice. The lack of rigour belongs to the tradition of 
the area, so it is not fair to criticize the book in this respect, except perhaps that 
it has contributed to the persistency of rule-of-thumb approaches. As everybody 
knows, it is especially the explanatory analysis of time series data that is difficult 
to handle rigourously; this is in particular the case in problems of significance 
analysis and hypothesis testing. And, as everybody also knows, the protagonists 
of interdependent systems have made laudable efforts to develop rigorous methods 
in the construction of explanatory econometric models. (Alas, it has fallen upon 
the reviewer to argue that there is something fundamentally doubtful in the 
interdependent approach.) 
With regard to the foundations of regression analysis, however, it is possible 
to remove the traditional lack of rigour in the treatment of nonexperimental data. 
Briefly stated, the argument is that once an explanatory relation is assumed to 
constitute a conditional expectation subject to random disturbance, this hypo- 
thesis provides in one stroke (a) the rationale for making operative use of the 
relation for predictive purposes, and (b) the rationale for parameter estimation 
by the use of least squares regression!. Although very simple, and although it must 
have been intuitively in the minds of many adepts of least squares methods, it 
would seem that the argument is new, at least in explicit form, and it so happens 
that it was published almost exactly at the same time as the edition under review. 
Thenew argument supports the basic approach of the treatise, and does not in essence 
change anything in the single-relation techniques and applied procedures. When 
it comes to the treatment of interdependent systems and other multirelation 
models, on the other hand, the argument has a deepgoing impact on model 
construction. 
With Karl A. Fox entering as joint author, the third edition is largely rewritten, 
and new material is added. The main change shows up in the title, namely the 
shift of emphasis from correlation to regression analysis. Hence “‘Correlation is not 
the same as causation’’—a famous old dictum and halftruth—may serve as motto 
for the two first editions, and ‘Least squares regression is under a very wide 
range of circumstances an appropriate method for the assessment of causal 
relationships,” for the present edition. The text has been rearranged and grouped 
in seven major sections. The new or very much enlarged chapters include Ch. 17 
on standard errors in multiple regression, Ch. 20 on error formulas for time series 
analysis?, Ch. 23 on the analysis of variance in its relation to regression problems,

same argument to multiple-relation models, see also papers by the reviewer in Econo- 
metrica, 27, pp. 443-63 and (forthcoming) in Bulletin of the International Statistical 
Institute, 38. 
2 On p. 335 the reviewer’s formula for standard errors of regression coefficients in

apparent that the advanced problems at issue cannot be discussed adequately at

University Institute of Statistics, Uppsala HERMAN O. A. WoLpD 
Introduction a la Théorie des Probabilités. Pak B. V. GNEDENKO ET A. Ia. 
KHINTCHINE. (Traduit par MARC GAILLARD). Paris: Dunod. 1960. viii + 157 pp. 
ACCORDING TO the translator’s preface, Gnedenko and Khintchine prepared this 
elementary probability text as a contribution to Russia’s war effort in the second 
world war. The many illustrative examples are therefore largely drawn from the 
military and manufacturing areas, and from the physical sciences. 
The book is intended to ‘introduce the reader to the fundamental concepts 
of probability theory;”’ it is aimed at a wide audience, and requires no calculus. 
The first part of the book, entitled Probabilities, deals with the concept of 
probability, the addition rule, conditional probabilities and the multiplication 
tule, Bayes’ theorem, the binomial distribution, and Bernoulli’s theorem. 
Probability is introduced informally: ‘‘The (proportion) of these ‘favorable’ 
results in a given sequence of uniform operations effectively is what we call the 
probability of the phenomenon considered,” and again: ‘‘We call probability the 
ratio of the number of ‘favorable’ results observed on the average, to the number of 
operations considered ;”’ the elementary manipulations of probability are justified 
at this informal level. 
Bayes’ theorem is presented as a tool for the exact re-evaluation of prior 
probabilities, which, in the authors’ words, are assumed known “‘in one way or 
another.’ This function of Bayes’ theorem is well illustrated by military, indus- 
trial, and medical examples. 
The remainder of the first part of the book is devoted to a discussion of the 
binomial distribution and a proof of Bernoulli’s theorem, the latter by Tcheby- 
chev’s inequality, using an argument paralleling the proof of the law of large 
numbers in the second part. Indeed, Tchebychev’s inequality seems generally to 
be singled out for special emphasis. 
The second part of the book, entitled Chance Variables, deals with chance 
variables and distributions, expectations and the expectation of sums and prod- 
ucts, measures of dispersion, the law of large numbers, and the normal distribu- 
tion. Proofs are given of the basic properties of expectations and variances, while 
the discussion of measures of dispersion comprises the mean deviation, the stan- 
dard deviation and the probable, or median, deviation. An unusual feature of the 
chapter on normal distributions is a proof of the fact that, for all these distribu- 
tions, the ratio of the standard to the probable deviation equals (0[2)"!s, which 
implies the additivity of squares of probable deviations of independent normal 
summands. 
the formula refers to the case where the residuals have zero intercorrelations (lagged 
and nonlagged) with the explanatory variables.

ae 
BOOK REVIEWS 259 
Notably excluded from this excellent book are dice, coins, and playing cards. 
The central limit theorem, called Liapounov’s theorem, is mentioned in the 
chapter on normality, and explained in the Conclusion. In closing, the reader is 
alerted to this translation’s sometimes surprising usage of the phrase ‘‘deux-a- 
deux indépendants,”’ for example on pages 99 and 152.
