Theory of Value. PAR G. DEBREU. Cowles Foundation. J. Wiley and Sons. 1959. 
114 pp. 
L’OUVRAGE DE G. Debreu constitue un nouvel exposé de la théorie de |’équilibre 
économique; mais cet exposé comporte vis-a-vis de ses devanciers de nombreuses 
innovations. 
La premiére est l’usage de la méthode axiomatique grace 4 laquelle les notions 
concrétes sont traduites en propositions mathématiques précises. Cette utilisation 
de la méthode axiomatique s’accompagne naturellement d’un souci de rigueur 
qui n’est pas habituel en matiére de théorie économique. C’est ainsi que l’on 
trouvera une démonstration de l’existence d’une fonction d’utilité continue pour 
chaque consommateur a partir de l’existence d’un préordre complet et d’une 
hypothése de continuité, et surtout des conditions d’existence d’un équilibre, d’un 
optimum et les conditions d’équivalence entre optimum et équilibre. 
Un second trait original est l’abandon des procédés du calcul différentiel au 
profit de la théorie des ensembles et l'utilisation des propriétés topologiques de 
lespace R™ des nombres réels 4 m dimensions et de ses sous-ensembles convexes. 
A cet effet un premier chapitre est consacré aux notions mathématiques nécessai- 
res a la compréhension des démonstrations qui suivent. 
La troisiéme originalité vient de la maniére de décrire les biens. Un bien n’est 
pas seulement décrit par les caractéres physiques mais aussi par un lieu et une 
date de disponibilité de sorte que la théorie présentée peut étre a la fois une 
généralisation de la théorie de l’intérét et d’une théorie économique des trans- 
ports. 
La quatriéme innovation introduite au chapitre VII est un renforcement de la 
précédente; il ne suffit plus pour décrire un “‘bien’’ ou un “service” d’en indiquer 
les caractéristiques physiques le lieu et la date de disponibilité, il faut préciser 
encore les conditions auxquelles cette disponibilité peut se trouver soumise. 
L’exemple que donne M. Debreu pour faire comprendre ce nouveau point de vue 
rend évident le caractére inadéquat des termes de “‘biens,” ‘‘services,’’ ou ‘‘mar- 
chandises.” I] vaudrait mieux parler d’obligations au sens juridique du mot: 
obligation de fournir (ou inversement droit de recevoir) une certaine quantité 
d’un bien (un sens habituel du terme) a4 une certaine date, en un certain lieu, si 
un certain événement s’est produit. Ainsi la valeur est une caractéristique non 
d’un élément matériel mais bien d’un élément juridique. 
Soucieux de préciser son propos, l’auteur n’a pas hésité a expliciter les points 
qu’il n’avait pas traité, mais en revanche il n’a donné aucune indication quant 
aux conséquences de sa théorie qui sont laissées a l’appréciation du lecteur. Ces 
conséquences sont cependant, 4 mon avis, trop importantes pour les passer sous 


--- Page 152 ---

260 BOOK REVIEWS 
silence et trop nombreuses pour les énumérer toutes. Citons cependant celle-ci: 
Le dernier chapitre a fait apparaitre l’existence de ‘“‘marchandises” d’un nouveau 
type pour lesquelles il n’existe pas de marché (en général tout au moins) et dés 
lors les prix de ces ‘‘marchandises”’ ne sauraient étre tenues pour fixes par les 
agents économiques qui ne les connaissent méme pas. Ainsi l’une des conditions 
d’application de la théorie de 1’équilibre au monde réel s’effondre. 
Gentilly, France E. BAUDIER 
Volkwirtschaftliche Gesamtrechnung. By WILHELM KRELLE. Berlin: Duncker 
& Humbolt. 1959. 158 pp. 
WITH THIS book Germany joins the club of countries which can boast an input- 
output table as well as national accounts. The table, 46 x 46, for the year 1953 
is the most striking feature of Professor Krelle’s excellent book. It was prepared 
by a team of nine co-workers out of research institutes at Heidelberg and Essen. 
The art of table construction and the data available for the enterprise still lag 
behind the promise of what such tables might uncover, were they available 
annually over a period of years. It is particularly unfortunate that lack of funds 
prevented publication of the projected second volume which would have given 
the detail on individual industries. In the present state of statistics, industry 
studies running back over time offer the nearest approach to a “‘dynamized”’ 
Leontief table. The publication of industry detail might have given us some 
interesting insights into the German economy in 1953—the product-mix within 
agriculture, for example, and the variations in input-output relationships between 
portions of an industry, if not among the firms themselves. 
Even a single table, however, permits some comparisons with the United 
States, and, if other tables were constructed for earlier or later years, with German 
experience itself. Besides the table, Professor Krelle has produced an excellent 
exposition of the concepts of national economic accounting, beginning with 
Kretislauftheorie and synthesizing with the aggregative accounts the input-output 
system in several variants. This part of the book contains, so far as I am aware, 
nothing new, but the restatement of Marx in these terms is especially interesting. 
The book thus contains something for both the foreign and the domestic consumer, 
and stands as a good indication of the state of German econometrics. 
University of North Carolina WILLIAM N. PARKER 
Impact Multipliers and Dynamic Properties of the Klein-Goldberger Model. By 
ARTHUR S. GOLDBERGER. Amsterdam: North-Holland Publishing Company. 1959. 
138 pp. $ 4.00. 
THE KLEIN-GOLDBERGER model was published in 1955 and has received a grati- 
fying amount of attention as the latest in the series of large aggregate dynamic 
econometric models of the United States economy. Professor Goldberger now 
reports on his testing of this model. 
This little book is a pleasure to read because the material is very carefully 


--- Page 153 ---

o= & Ww wows 6 
~ - Oo 
BOOK REVIEWS 261 
presented with tables and charts adequately explained. Unfortunately it has no 
index, which is certainly a weakness in a book of this kind. 
The tests are of five different kinds, one of which is, of course, the fit of the 
model in the sample period, 1929-41, 1946-52. The war years have been omitted. 
There is a substantial increase in the number of post-war years over Klein’s 
earlier model, and there is substantial improvement. With 1958 data available 
we shall have as many post-war years as there are pre-war years in the Klein- 
Goldberger model; and with each passing year we find ourselves a little closer to 
large sample stability. Another long stride forward will be taken when quarterly 
data are used rather than annual, and we can have much more flexible time lags. 
Together with the improvement which this model shows over earlier ones, we 
should expect much of the future. 
The second kind of testing is forecasting. In this respect the model under 
review has done very well, even remarkably well, in view of the weaknesses of 
previous models. The monetary variables of the model, iowever, have performed 
somewhat less well than the real variables, a point which is further discussed 
below. Forecasts are made for the years 1953, 1954, and 1955, and tests are made 
with respect to the direction of change as well as the level of the variables. The 
Klein-Goldberger model does better than the “‘naive’’ models in each of the three 
years. 
The third kind of testing is that of the estimated values of the coefficients of 
the equations. The sampling errors were not computed because of the complexity 
of the work, and it was felt that the results would be ambiguous. This is unfortu- 
nate, because we should like to know just how ambiguous they are in view of the 
general success of the present work. One of the areas of study is the question of 
how appropriate probability theory is to these models; or whether Frisch’s con- 
fluence analysis is suitable, after all. 
The tests of this section consisted in examining the coefficients of the reduced 
forms, that is the estimate of the immediate effects of the predetermined variables 
upon the endogenous variables, which coefficients are here named ‘‘impact multi- 
pliers.”” The next step is to consider the intermediate run responses, that is the 
effects over a few years, usually seven. Finally, the last chapter is devoted to the 
dynamic characteristics of the model. 
This last chapter begins with a general mathematical discussion because there 
was until the new edition of Baumol’s book no adequate presentation in the 
general literature. The model is then divided into two parts, the real and the 
monetary part. Each part model is found to be dynamically stable, that is, over 
the years it would tend to settle down to a steady state. The monetary sector, 
however, is rather close to the margin, so that some slight changes in the model 
might make it unstable. The real sector has a cyclical element which tends to be 
damped out. In another study by the Adelmans, published in Econometrica, Oct., 
1959 this stability is emphasized. They subjected the Klein-Goldberger model to 
a severe shock in the eighth year and then calculated the results out to the hund- 
redth year (with appropriate estimates of the exogenous variables). 
The work of aggregate model building is showing enough promise that we 
should encourage it as much as possible. We need to know more about the results 


--- Page 154 ---

262 BOOK REVIEWS 
of different estimation procedures, and in particular the use of probability. On 
these scores the Klein-Goldberger work is a little disappointing. On ingenuity and 
patient thoroughness of their work they get high marks. 
McGill University E. F. BEACH 
Delay in the Court. An Analysis of the Remedies for Delayed Justice. By Hans 
ZEISEL, HARRY KALVEN, JR., AND BERNARD BUCHHOLZ. Boston: Little, Brown 
and Company. 1959. xxvii + 313 pp. $ 7.00. 
THIS BOOK is the first of a series by the University of Chicago Law School which 
will report on Ford Foundation researches on the jury and judicial administration. 
The authors have limited their study to a single court system—the Supreme Court 
of New York County. It is not expected that the conclusions will apply accurately 
to other courts; nevertheless the book will undoubtedly serve as a useful point 
of departure for future studies of other courts. 
After giving attention to various ways of measuring delay, the authors find 
that there is a nearly static backlog of 11.7 judge years. A rather encouraging 
conclusion emerges: A twelve per cent increase in judge power (i.e., two judges) 
would remove the backlog in six years. 
Having measured the delay, three broad classes of remedies are considered: 
(1) reducing the trial time, (2) increasing settlements, and (3) more judge time. 
In each class a number of possibilities are studied in detail. For example, there is 
the highly controversial issue of shifting certain cases from jury to bench in order 
to save trial time. The question of how much time could thus be saved is fraught 
with statistical pitfalls. A fairly obvious one: Since jury trials average 17.4 hours 
and bench trials 8.9 hours, jury cases could be tried in about half the time if they 
were shifted to the bench. By rather more sophisticated arguments the authors 
get three independent (and surprisingly consistent) estimates—36, 45, and 40 
percent—for the saving in trial time if an average personal injury case were 
shifted from jury to bench. 
Another interesting and controversial issue is raised in Chapter 21, ‘“The Case 
for the Official Experiment.”’ Here is the authors’ summary: ‘‘The most precise 
way of measuring the differential effects of alternative solutions for an administra- 
tive problem is the controlled experiment. Since it involves the inclusion of 
randomly selected cases in the experiment, and the exclusion of others, the 
official legal experiment poses a problem of equal treatment under the law. The 
legal and moral aspects are discussed.”’ Statisticians will, I think, generally agree 
that evidence from a controlled experiment would supply more reliable conclusions 
than those based on observational evidence of the kind that the authors have 
been obliged to use. In support of the case for experimentation there is a quotation 
from Mr. Justice Brandeis and reference to an experimental study of parole 
policies in California. A minor slip on p. 246: “If... the experiment should not 
reveal any difference in the two treatments, any claim of discrimination is 
authoritatively disproved.’’ One must, I think, admit the possibility of a small 
treatment difference which the experiment was not sufficiently sensitive to detect. 


--- Page 155 ---

ae eS Se Se OS 
BOOK REVIEWS 263 
The book is well organized, clearly written, indexed, and adequately docu- 
mented. There are 100 figures and tables; the figures are agreeably simple and 
neatly drawn. 
Iowa State University ROBERT J. BUEHLER 
Economic Dynamics. An Introduction. By Wit1t1aAm J. Baumo. New York: 
The Macmillan Company. Second Edition. 1959. 369 pp. 
THIS REPRINT, after eight years, of a classic work, hardly altered in its economic 
parts though enlarged by some very valuable new mathematical chapters, forces 
one to ponder, like Hamlet, the vanity of all wordly things. By rereading this 
pioneering effort in the field of Economic Growth and Dynamics we realise how 
far the surge of research has enlarged our insights in the mere handful of years 
since the first introduction of this book to the public in 1951. Professor Baumol 
must have stood before the alternative of completely ‘‘rebuilding”’ this book or of 
touching up its arguments to bring them into line with present knowledge. He 
wisely chose the second alternative though the touching up of the argument has, 
to my taste, been perhaps a little too slight. This book’s Introduction and Part I; 
Magnificent Dynamics (the formulation of growth theories of Ricardo, Marx, etc.) 
will always be a classic. But the sentence, ‘“‘We consider these older dynamics 
simply because, although imperfect, they represent an approach of which there 
are few recent examples,” (p. 13) should perhaps no longer have been written, 
after the works of Leibenstein and Hamberg and to a lesser degree Haavelmo and 
Kaldor. Statements on the consumption function (p. 41) and the narrowness of 
the growth path (p. 54) also do not seem to take sufficient account of Duesenberry 
and Friedman, on the one hand, or Solow, on the other. The author’s forte in this 
book has, above all, been (and still is) his pedagogic simplifications, but these 
might be considered to involve a certain, inevitable lack of rigor. One could, for 
instance, take umbrage with formulations such as calling the Multiplier-Accelera- 
tor model a ‘‘model... that concerns (itself with) what will actually happen to 
income.”’ And finally, what I have always considered one of the main weaknesses 
of Baumol’s terminology, namely calling an absolute increment and not a relative 
change in a variable its rate of change, has been eliminated only very partially 
by adding a new footnote on page 42. Since students notoriously overread foot- 
notes I fear that, by reading the economic parts of the book under review, they 
will not learn one of the basic prerequisites of all growth theory, the thinking in 
terms of relative rather than absolute change. The perhaps too slight effort in 
revising this volume can be further evidenced by saying that only five new titles 
(10%) have been added to the reading list of the first three parts, and including 
those concerning the new Parts IV and V only one quarter (31 out of 124) of the 
titles in the whole list have not shown up in the first edition. 
What has perhaps been neglected in the mainly economic parts of the second 
edition is amply recouped by the great enlargement of the mainly mathematical 
chapters. Nowhere can there be found such a complete and yet elementary treat- 
ment of difference equations. New are especially the qualitative evaluations of 
the roots of difference equations, for instance by Sturm’s method (as well as a 


--- Page 156 ---

264 BOOK REVIEWS 
treatment of the Newton approximation of the roots of equations). The concepts 
of stability and stationary equilibrium (the constant particular solution of the 
equation) are more fully worked out in their mathematical terms. In the treat- 
ment of differential equations qualitative evaluation is again introduced, for here 
as throughout the volume the correspondence between difference and differential 
equations is stressed and duly capped by an elementary treatment of the algebra 
of operators. Furthermore, Baumol presents for the first time an appreciably 
simple treatment of nonlinear differential systems, a field in which he has done 
much original research. His great pedagogic trick in the mathematical chapters 
is the simultaneous use in the argument of general and numerical equations. In 
conclusion, one can say that the mathematical chapters of this book have been 
enlarged so greatly (from 40% to 60% of the pages) that the book might now even 
more justly be called ‘Difference and Differential Equations for Economists 
(knowing hardly any Mathematics).’”’ As such, however, it can today no longer 
give a self-contained course in Economic Dynamics (or Growth) but should rather 
precede it as a methodological introduction. 
Universitat Wien ERICH STREISSLER 
Studies in the Economics of Welfare Maximization. By P. R. BRAHMANANDA. 
Bombay: Bombay University Press. 1959. 518 pp. Rs. 18. 
THIS BOOK is mainly an adaptation of Marshallian economics to the development 
of underdeveloped areas; there is a separable section on relevant Classical analysis. 
The assumed objective is increase in production; distribution is assumed to be 
of interest almost exclusively for its effects on future production. The author 
recommends government encouragement or creation of large firms, as well as 
extensive restriction of freedom of individual choice in production and consumption. 
The author thinks that the underdeveloped areas are characterized by availabi- 
lity of significant economies of scale, and that the time required for growth of 
well managed small firms may delay development intolerably. Timing is also 
crucial in connection with the introduction of new machinery, which should be 
withheld until displaced laborers can be re-employed without psychological 
damage due to either duration of unemployment or change in kind of work. 
It may be necessary to limit conspicuous consumption and to restrict the range 
of choice among goods and grades of goods. Sanctions may be needed to enforce 
full use of resources and to limit population replacement. 
In an underdeveloped area, resource shifts must be extensive. The author re- 
commends evaluation of such potential reorganizations by use of a calculus of 
consumer’s and producer’s surplus, with broad grouping of individuals. 
The author’s general approach to the development problem seems reasonable. 
The methods he advocates seem appropriate for bringing about an acceptable rate 
of development; the valid objections against his procedures are important princi- 
pally in relation to marginal adjustments in highly developed countries. His 
technical analysis is competent. 
The book has no empirical content. Except for incidental references to Indian 
conditions, it does not pertain to any specified underdeveloped area. Thus the 


--- Page 157 ---

BOOK REVIEWS 265 
reader is left with the vague impression that the author’s substantive suggestions 
ought to be considered when development plans are made. 
The separable section on the Classicals (particularly Ricardo) is well done. It 
contributes to fuller appreciation of the merits of Ricardo’s analysis, and of its 
relevance for current development problems.

Universidad de Madrid ANGEL ALCAIDE INCHAUSTI 
Linear programming methods and application. By Saut I. Gass. McGraw-Hill 
Book Company, Inc. 1958. vii-+-223 pp. 
OF THE many books that have emerged on the mathematics of and computational 
techniques for linear programming this is certainly one of the most rigorous and 
useful. The preface to the book is an excellent thumbnail sketch of the history 
of programming methods and the bibliographies at the end of the book are some 
of the most useful that the reader has yet encountered. The bibliographies are 
divided into a section on applications and a list of basic references to linear pro- 
gramming methods. The book is reasonably self contained in that the necessary 
theory of matrices, determinants, vectors, convex sets, linear inequalities, and 
simultaneous linear equations is contained in Chapter 2. This chapter, however, is 
only 25 pages long and it is doubtful that students without considerable mathe- 
matical sophistication would find it adequate. On the other hand, for him who 
has been exposed to but forgotten the details of matrices, vectors, convex sets, 
and the like, this is an invaluable summary chapter. 


--- Page 159 ---

BOOK REVIEWS 267 
In Part II of the book, the theory of programming is presented and computa- 
tional procedures developed. The reviewer found the descriptions of the computa- 
tional procedures very complete and clear. 
Part III is devoted to applications. A transportation problem and solution is 
developed and a reasonably exhaustive discussion of the various special types of 
programming problems yet encountered is presented. This book seems to be 
remarkably free from printing errors, although on page 130 a title has the word 
“coeds” substituted for the word ‘‘codes’’. 
It is probably useful in a review to compare a given book with its recent com- 
petitors. Two other books on linear programming, Linear Programming Methods 
by Heady and Candler, and Elementary Mathematical Programming by Robert 
W. Metzger, have been published recently. The Heady and Candler book is much 
longer than the Gass book but less rigorous mathematically. The Heady and 
Candler book contains many completely worked out concrete problems, whereas 
the Gass book presents applications in an abstract and symbolic manner. The 
Metzger book seems to be designed for readers with almost no mathematical 
sophistication and very limited knowledge of economics. This reader would guess 
that most users would find the cross elasticities between the Gass book and the 
Heady book to be positive but small, and between the Gass work and the Metzger 
work to be zero. 
One of the book’s chief merits is the consistent and the relatively simple notation 
employed. This can be a very useful book for use in courses at the graduate 
level on linear programming for students who have some mathematical sophistica- 
tion and some knowledge of either economics or business administration. It is 
probably too abstract and compressed, however, to be useful to students without 
this particular combination of preparation. This book will undoubtedly find wide 
use as a reference book and as a text book for graduate courses in the field.

Strategy and Market Structure: Competition, Oligopoly, and the Theory of Games. 
By MartTIN SHuBIK. New York: John Wiley and Sons. 1959. 387 pp. $8.00. 
SIXTEEN YEARS after publication of the first edition of von Neumann and Morgen- 
stern’s Theory of Games and Economic Behavior, Shubik’s is the first book fully 
devoted to the detailed application of game theory to economic analysis. It 
deals mainly with duopoly, oligopoly, and related market forms. 
Part I, among other things, contains an interesting restatement of Cournot’s, 
Bertrand’s and Edgeworth’s duoply models in modern game-theoretical terms, 
showing that they represent non-cooperative games. It also gives a critical discuss- 
ion of Professor Chamberlin’s and Mrs. Robinson’s theories of monopolistic and of 
imperfect competition; and briefly explains the von Neumann-Morgenstern and 
the Nash theories of duopoly, based on the concept of cooperative games. There are 
many instructive remarks on the mathematical properties of the various duopoly 
models and on their interrelations. 
While Part I deals mainly with static theories and keeps the discussion on the 
rather abstract level usual in mathematical economics, Part [l—which expands 


--- Page 160 ---

268 BOOK REVIEWS 
the author’s own theory of duopoly and oligopoly—introduces dynamic consider- 
ations and institutional factors. His theory is based on a particular type of non- 
cooperative game, which he calls games of economic survival. They are a 
modification of games of military survival, developed by Hausner, Peisakoff, 
Shapley, and Milnor at RAND Corporation (which again are a generalization of 
the classical gambler’s ruin problem). In a game of economic survival two or 
more players (firms) compete with one another in the same market over an inde- 
finite number of periods. In each period their market behavior can be represented 
by a given (usually non-zero-sum) subgame. By threats of retaliations (price wars, 
advertising wars, blocking of supplies, etc.), the stronger players may be able to 
force the weaker players to adopt collusive policies, or even to withdraw from the 
market, or to reirain from entering it. The model allows introduction of a good 
deal of institutional information. Depending on the assumptions about corporate 
structure and financial arrangements, the firms of the model may be assumed 
to maximize their distributed profits (discounted to their present value) or the 
probability of their survival, or their rate of growth, etc., and the implications of 
each assumption can be investigated. Again, each firms’s ability to make or to 
resist threats will depend on its financial position, dividend policy, liquidation 
value, etc. 
In spite of the author’s skill and ingenuity in developing his model, his treat- 
ment clearly shows the limitations of his present approach, which bases the 
theory of duopoly and oligopoly on a non-cooperative game model. Unlike the 
various duopoly models based on Nash’s theory of two-person cooperative 
games (bargaining games) discussed in Part I of Shubik’s book, and unlike the 
oligopoly models based on this reviewer’s -person generalization of Nash’s theory, 
Shubik’s own model does not supply determinate predictions on how the market 
will be divided between the different duopolists or oligopolists—though to supply 
such predictions seems to be une of the principal tasks of a theory of duopoly or 
oligopoly. The model’s failure to supply such predictions also greatly restricts its 
quantitative, empirically testable implications. Moreover, whereas in Nash’s 
theory the criterion for distinguishing between admissible and inadmissible threats 
directly follows from certain general postulates of rational behavior, Shubik in 
his own model has to adopt an arbitrary ad hoc criterion for this purpose. 
Finally, some minor points. On p. 71, strictly speaking, the “‘worst profit that 
the second (firm) could impose upon the first’ is given by the first firm’s minimax 
profit, and not by its maximin profit as stated in the text (though quantitatively 
of course these two expressions are equal in most cases of interest). On p. 240 in 
the first equation P; should be replaced by R;,¢; and afterwards, in the rest of 
Section 5.1, Ps,¢ should always be replaced by Rg, ¢. 
All in all, despite the objections that may be raised to the author’s model, 
this book is a valuable pioneering contribution to the economic applications of 
game theory and to what the author rightly calls ‘mathematical institutional 
economics.”’ 
Joun C. HAPSANYI The Australian National University

BOOK REVIEWS 269 
The Logic of Investment Planning. By S. Coakravarty. Amsterdam: North- 
Holland Publishing Company. 1959. xii +- 170 pp. 
THIS STUDY EXAMINES the highly important problem of multi-sector investment 
planning for economic development. It does so with the methods of building 
models of economic policy and with the proficiency now expected from studies 
undertaken at the Netherlands Economic Institute. 
After a slow and protracted beginning which merely reviews some methodological 
issues and summarizes the Harrod-Domar, Mahalanobis, Leontief, and von Neu- 
mann models of an expanding economy, the discussion finally becomes more 
inspired as it attempts to extend these models in several ways. Through successive 
models of growth processes, the author incorporates gestation lags between invest- 
ment expenditure and an increase in productive capacity, introduces the compli- 
cation of international trade, allows for the emergence of new industries, and 
includes changes in relative prices. The final result is a multi-sector (multi-commodi- 
ty) dynamic model which may be used to draw inferences about the “optimal” 
commodity-composition of investment. “Optimal’’ refers to the investments need- 
ed to realise a certain rate and composition of growth, as specified by policy deci- 
sions. Solution of the model is by numerical extrapolation. 
Of the various refinements introduced by the author, that dealing with the prob- 
lem of “‘structural break’ could have been most illuminating. For there is cer- 
tainly a need to consider how, in the course of industrialization, an investment 
program may convert a previously “empty sector’ into a producing sector. But 
the actual treatment of this problem is disappointing. To incorporate the phenom- 
enon of discontinuous variation, the model must proceed through qualitatively 
different phases in which the number of variables and the corresponding number 
of degrees of freedom change. The problem is interpreted, however, as additional 
restrictions of the system, caused by a decision external to the system, and not 
as a result of the inner working of the system itself. Further, the concern is with the 
implications of a structural break, rather than with the more fundamental 
problem of the reasons for introducing a structural break and the decisions concern- 
ing its character, magnitude, and timing. 
This superficial treatment of a structural break is symptomatic of the study’s 
general weakness: by treating investment magnitudes as exogenously prescribed 
by the planning authority, there is no allowance for endogenous investment decisions 
and there is no insight into the basic issue of determining the investment criteria 
in the first place. Instead of pointing up the actual choice of investment criteria, 
the procedure is simply a mechanical one of cranking out solutions for assumed 
preference functions of the planning authority. The result is that, although the final 
model is clearly the work of a fine craftsman, its external relevance is limited by 
the author’s own statement that his “‘way of looking at the problem has practical 
significance if we can assume that it is empirically possible to determine a social wel- 
fare function, and estimate in a certain concrete situation the numerical values of 
the coefficients occurring in the welfare function.”’ This assumption conceals the 
most significant substantive issues, and the development economist must still look 
elsewhere for some answers to the challenging question of investment criteria. 
Wesleyan University GERALD M. MEIER 


--- Page 162 ---

270 BOOK REVIEWS 
La Funzione d’Imprenditore e la sua Offerta. By ERcoLE Moroni. Milano: 
Dott. A. Giuffre - Editore. 1958. 114 pp. Lire 800. 
THE Score OF this study, according to the author, is to bring to light some of 
the elements that determine the supply of entrepreneurs. Most of the book, 
however, is dedicated to a rather thorough survey of the economic and institutional 
implications of the term entrepreneur (Chapters II and III) in order to determine 
logically whether the entrepreneurial function can be treated as a factor of 
production (Chapter IV). The author first distinguishes between the entrepreneur 
per se (in the Schumpeterian sense) and the entrepreneur whose services are 
nothing more than specialized and highly remunerated labor. He then concludes 
that the entrepreneurial services per se (i.e., those ultimate strategical decisions 
which, under conditions of uncertainty, the entrepreneur cannot delegate to em- 
ployees, even those of higher rank) cannot be defined as a factor of production in 
the same sense that land, capital, or labor can. On the other hand, the entrepre- 
neurial services in the second sense constitute a scarce resource with the same 
theoretical problems as other factors of production (p. 88). 
The last chapter of the book (Chapter V) explores a relatively new field: the 
supply curve of entrepreneurs for each industry. The author using an idea first 
presented by Mrs. Joan Robinson develops a short-run and a long-run supply 
curve which is of some theoretical interest (p. 95 ff). 
The author’s model of entrepreneurship, unfortunately, may not be applicable 
in fields like agricultural economics in which a great deal of research is being done 
on production functions fitted to cross-sectional data. If management fer se 
cannot be considered as a factor of production, there is little hope of correcting 
supposed “‘biases’’ in estimates derived from these production functions. The 
author reasons that entrepreneurship cannot be counted a factor of production 
because it is the entrepreneur himself who chooses among several combinations of 
inputs and outputs and could hardly include himself as a factor of production. 
However, we consider that this difficulty disappears if an ex post approach is 
used: the entrepreneurial ability can then be reflected in the differential returns 
of single enterprises. It is not impossible to conceive the computation of a manage- 
ment index. This index could be incorporated into the production function as a 
new variable provided it is not correlated with the other factors of production. 
Little empirical work has been done on the subject, however. Its computation 
undoubtedly presents certain complexities since to avoid perfect correlation with 
the other inputs (obtained from farm records) other additional variables such as 
age, experience of farmer as a manager, capital equity and risk aversion, education, 
etc. should be tried. 
This work offers a fair review of the economic and institutional aspects of the 
concept of the entrepreneur, although it does not deal with any of its econometric 
implications. The bibliography, which includes some European sources not too 
frequently mentioned in our circles, might be of interest to economic theorists and 
researchers. 
Iowa State University GONZALO ARROYO

ANNOUNCEMENTS AND NOTES 
ANNOUNCEMENT OF THE DECEMBER, 1961 MEETINGS 
The December, 196! meeting of the Econometric Society will be held jointly 
with the American Economic Association, the American Farm Economic Associa- 
tion, the American Statistical Association, and other allied social science associa- 
tions in New York City. Professor Marc Nerlove of Stanford University is Chairman 
of the Program Committee. Other members of the committee include Irma Adelman, 
Stanford University; Allen R. Ferguson, The RAND Corporation; Franklin M. 
Fisher, Massachusetts Institute of Technology; Martin J. Gerra, U.S. Department 
of Agriculture; Arthur Goldberger, University of Wisconsin; Edwin S. Mills, 
Johns Hopkins University and Cowles Foundation for Research in Economics; 
Jacob Mincer, Columbia University; Herbert Scarf, Stanford University; Robert 
Summers, University of Pennsylvania; and Lester G. Telser, University of Chicago. 
The ex officio members of the committee are John R. Meyer, Harvard University, 
Chairman of the December, 1960 meetings; T. C. Liu, Cornell University, Chairman 
of the August, 1961 meeting; Franco Modigliani, Northwestern University, Vice- 
President of the Econometric Society; and Richard Ruggles, Yale University, 
Secretary of the Econometric Society. F. Thomas Juster, National Bureau of 
Economic Research, 261 Madison Avenue, New York 16, is in charge of local 
arrangements. Abstracts and titles of proposed papers should be sent to any 
member of the program committee except ex officio members. 
ANNOUNCEMENT OF THE EUROPEAN MEETING 
The Econometric Society will hold its annual European meeting at Paris, 
France on August 28-30, 1961. The members of the program committee are: 
René Roy, Paris, Chairman; Ragnar Bentzel, Uppsala; James Durbin, London; 
C. J. van Eijk, The Hague; Wilhelm Krelle, Bonn; Siro Lombardini, Milan. Two 
joint sessions will be held, one on Wednesday August 30, with the International 
Statistical Institute, the other on Monday August 28, with The Institute of Mana- 
gement Sciences, Mr. Claude Fourgeaud, Service des Etudes Economiques et 
Financiéres, 6 avenue de l’Opéra, Paris, will be in charge of local arrangements. 
ANNOUNCEMENT OF THE SUMMER MEETING 
The summer meeting of the Econometric Society will be held this year from 
August 29-September | at Stillwater, Oklahoma. Professor Ta-Chung Liu, Cornell 
University, is Chairman of the Program Committee. Other members of the com- 
mittee include Martin J. Bailey, University of Chicago; John A. Brittain, 
Vanderbilt University; Arthur S. Goldberger, University of Wisconsin; Donald 
F. Gorden, University of Washington; Benjamin Higgins, University of Texas; 
Ira Horowitz, Indiana University; Leonid Hurwicz, University of Minnesota; 
Dale W. Jorgenson, University of California, Berkeley; George G. Judge, Uni- 
versity of Illinois; and Edwin S. Mills, Johns Hopkins University and Cowles 
Foundation. E. J. R. Booth of Oklahoma State University is in charge of local 
arrangements.

ACCOUNTANTS’ OPINION 
Members of The Econometric Society: 
We have examined the balance sheet of The Econometric Society as of Septem- 
ber 30, 1960 and the related statement of income and expense for the year then 
ended. Our examination was made in accordance with generally accepted auditing 
standards, and accordingly included such tests of the accounting records and such 
other auditing procedures as we considered necessary in the circumstances. 
In our opinion, the accompanying balance sheet and statement of income and 
expense present fairly the financial position of the Society at September 30, 1960 
and the results of its operations for the year then ended, in conformity with 
generally accepted accounting principles applied on a basis consistent with that 
of the preceding year. Haskins & SELLS 
New Haven, December 14, 1960

ANNOUNCEMENTS AND NOTES 275 
NEWS NOTES 
Travel grants will be made to a number of mathematicians who wish to attend 
the International Congress of Mathematicians in Stockholm on August 15-22, 
1962. It is hoped that funds available through various sources may provide travel 
assistance for a considerable number of mathematicians. 
There will be a greater effort than in the past to give aid to younger people. 
As grants will be made only to those who have filed applications, it is urgent that 
any who wish to receive a grant should fill out and file an application. Younger 
people are urged to file applications so that their cases can be considered. 
Applications can be obtained from the Division of Mathematics, National 
Academy of Sciences, National Research Council, Washington 25, D. C., by 
requesting an application for a travel grant to the 1962 International Congress. 
The deadline for filing of applications is November 1, 1961, and an attempt 
will be made to announce the grants by January 1, 1962. 
Awarding of grants will be made only to those persons whose applications have 
been received, in good order, by November 1. The selection will be made by a 
committee consisting of the regular Committee on Travel Grants of the Division 
of Mathematics of the National Academy of Sciences—National Research Council 
enlarged to include representatives of societies affiliated with the Division and 
representatives of various governmental agencies.

BOOKS 
Progress in Operation Research, Volume! 
Edited by RUSSELL L. ACKOFF, Case 
Institute of Technology. 
This book inaugurates a series of review 
volumes which will “inventory” the mathe- 
matical techniques and research methods 
available to operations researchers. The em- 
phasis of Volume I lies in the area of technical 
progress: in the development of modelling 
techniques and ways of using these to solve 
problems. The discussions cover eight im- 
portant phases of operations research — 
Inventory Theory, Linear Programming, 
Dynamic Programming, Queuing Theory, 
Sequencing Theory, Replacement Theory, 
Simulation, and Gaming. For each of these 
areas, an article is included which evaluates 
the current “state of the art,” surveys impor- 
tant applications, and discusses deficiencies 
and unsolved problems. (#5 in ORSA’s 
series on Publications in Operations Re- 
search.) 
1961. Approx. 480 pages. Prob. $ 9.75. 
Problems in Basic Operations Research 
Methods for 
By RANDOLPH W. CABELL, Interna- 
tional Business Machines Corporation; 
and ALMARIN PHILLIPS, University of 
Virginia. Consisting exclusively of problems 
(except for the brief introduction), this book 
contains neither text nor solutions; these are 
purposely avoided so that readers are required 
to search for a method of solution and to 
solve the problems without a specific guide 
to the “right” answer. 
Managements 
A point of emphasis in the book is the need 
for building models to fit problems, rather 
than applying ‘“cook-book” solutions. The 
problems given here, when used in conjunc 
tion with text material, offer a vehicle for 
the purpose of providing a full understanding 
of the methods. 
1961. 110 pages. 
Proceedings of the Second 
International Conference on Operational 
Research (Aix-en-Provence 1960) 
Organized by the International Federation 
of Operational Research Societies. Edited 
by John Banbury and John Maitland. 
These are the papers that were presented for 
discussion at this conference, at the Univer- 
sity of Aix-Marseille in Aix-en-Provence, 
from September 5 to 9, 1960. 
The articles are divided into two sections: 
Part I consists mainly of papers given in the 
plenary sessions, and Part II of papers on 
applications in certain fields. Each paper is 
in either English or French and a translated 
abstract appears at the end. 
In Press. 
Reserve your examination copies 
JOHN WILEY & SONS, Inc. 440 Park Avenue South, New York 16, N.Y.

$6.00 per year. Canada and Pan American Postal Union $6.50 per year. All other countries $7.00 per year. Single

Important 
M cGra w- HillBooks 
ECONOMETRICS: An Introduction to Maximum Likelihood 
Methods 
By STEFAN VALAVANIS, Harvard University. Edited from manuscript by ALFRED 
H. Conrap, Harvard University. 223 pages, $7.00. 
This short introduction to econometrics — the techniques by which economic theories are 
brought into contact with the facts — is especially addressed to problems of estimation, and 
its orientation is always toward the strategy of research. The maximum likelihood and 
limited information techniques are developed from fundamental assumptions and criteria, 
and are demonstrated by example. 
INTRODUCTION TO LABOR ECONOMICS, Third Edition 
By Orme W. PHELps, Claremont Men’s College. 566 pages, $7.50. 
Describes the principal forces at work in the U.S. labor market (employer, employees, 
unions, and government agencies) and the issues raised by their interaction. Over haif the 
book deals with labor unions: their history, organization, methods, legal status, and objec- 
tives. The remainder of the text discusses and analyzes the general character of the labor 
force and the labor market, the economics of wages, and the modern theory of employment. 
ECONOMICS: An Introductory Analysis, Fifth Edition 
By Paut A. SAMUELSON, Massachusetts Institute of Technology. 855 pages, $7.50. 
In this completely updated edition of Samuelson’s outstanding text, the author has com- 
pletely revised the Micro-Economics Section and has added new aids for the student and the 
teacher. As before, a remarkable combination of rigorous analysis and a singularly teachable 
approach. Ideal for a wide variety of introductory courses. 
INTRODUCTION TO STATISTICS FOR BUSINESS DECISIONS 
By Rospert Scuiairer, Harvard University. 382 pages, $7.75. 
An abridged and less mathematical version of Schlaifer’s brilliant and pioneering text 
PROBABILITY AND STATISTICS FOR BUSINESS DECISIONS. The primary objective 
of this text is to set forth as simply as possible the basic principles of the classical decision 
theory of Neyman and Pearson and show how this theory is completed rather than contra- 
dicted by recent developments based on the concepts of utility and personal probability. 
McGRAW-HILL BOOK COMPANY, INC. Send for copies on

THE ECONOMETRIC SOCIETY 
An International Society for the Advancement of Economic 
Theory in its Relation to Statistics and Mathematics 
[Founded December 29, 1930 
Scope of the Society 
Section 1 of the Constitution: ““The Econometric 
Society is an international society for the advance- 
ment of economic theory in its relation to statistics 
and mathematics. The Society shall operate as a 
completely disinterested, scientific organization 
without political, social, financial, or nationalistic 
bias. Its main object shall be to promote studies that 
aim at a unification of the theoretical-quantitative 
and the empirical-quantitative approach to econo- 
mic problems and that are penetrated by construc- 
tive and rigorous thinking similar to that which has 
come to dominate in the natural sciences. Any ac- 
tivity which promises ultimately to further such 
unification of theoretical and factual studies in eco- 
nomics shall be within the sphere of interest of the 
Society.” 
Membership 
Membership is open to anyone seriously interested 
in the objectives of the Society. Persons wishing to 
become candidates for membership should write to 
the Secretary of the Econometric Society, Box 1264, 
Yale Station, New Haven, Connecticut, U.S.A. for 
information about the Society and a membership 
application blank. 
Membership dues, including subscription to Eco- 
nometrica, are: For members residing in the United 
States and Canada, $10.00 per year; for members re- 
siding in all other countries, $6.50 per year. These 
dues should be forwarded to the office of the Trea- 
surer of the Econometric Society, Box 1264, Yale 
Station, New Haven, Connecticut, U.S.A. 
Econometrica 
EconoMETRICcA, the journal of the Society, ap- 
pears quarterly, in January, April, July, and Octo- 
ber. Members of the Society receive the journal free. 
Subscription to Econometrica for nonmembers, 
including libraries: $14.00 per year in the United 
States and Canada; all other countries, $12.00. Sin- 
gle copies, $4.00. Nonmember subscription rate for 
graduate students recommended by a faculty mem- 
ber, $6.00 per year. Subscriptions should be for- 
warded to the office of the Secretary of the Econo- 
metric Society, Box 1264, Yale Station, New Haven, 
Connecticut, U.S.A. All payments should be made 
in United States funds. 
Manuscripts intended for publication in Econo- 
METRICA should be sent, preferably in triplicate and 
double-spaced, to Robert H. Strotz, Managing Edi- 
tor of EcoNoMETRICA, The Department of Econo- 
mics, Northwestern University, Evanston, Illinois, 
U.S.A., or to M. Edmond Malinvaud, I.N.S.E.E., 
29 Quai Branly, Paris, France. 
But de la Société 
Article I des Statuts: ‘‘La Société d’Econométrie 
est une Société internationale pour l’avancement de 
la théorie économique dans ses rapports avec les sta- 
tistiques et les mathématiques. La Société doit fonc- 
tionner cormme une organisation purement scienti- 
fique et complétement désintéressée, sans aucune 
tendance politique, sociale, financiére, ou nationale. 
Son objet essentiel est de favoriser les études de 
caractére quantitatif qui tendent 4 rapprocher le 
point de vue théorique du point de vue empirique 
dans l’exploration des problémes économiques, et 
qui sont inspirés d’un esprit méthodique et rigou- 
reux—semblable a celui quia prévalu dans les scien- 
ces de la nature. Toute activité susceptible de fa- 
voriser, immédiatement ou plus tard, pareille uni- 
fication des études économiques théoriques et em- 
piriques, est du domaine de la Société.” 
Membres 
Toute personne qui s’intéresse sérieusement aux 
objets de la Société peut en devenir membre. Si elle 
désire poser sa candidature elle doit écrire au Secré- 
taire de la Société d’Econométrie, Box 1264, Yale 
Station, New Haven, Connecticut, U.S.A. qui lui 
enverra des renseignements sur la Société et un 
formulaire de candidature. 
Les cotisations des membres, comprenant l’abon- 
nement a Econometrica sont: pour les membres 
résidant aux Etats-Unis ou au Canada, 10 dollars 
par an; pour les membres résidant dans tous les au- 
tres pays, 6,50 dollars par an. Ces cotisations doivent 
étre versées au Bureau du Trésorier de la Société 
d’Econométrie, Box 1264, Yale Station, New 
Haven, Connecticut, U.S.A. 
Econometrica 
EcoNoMETRICA, le journal de la Société, parait 
quatre fois par an, en janvier, avril, juillet, et octo- 
bre. Les membres de la Société regoivent le journal 
gratuitement. L’abonnement ECONOMETRICA pour 
non-membres, y compris les bibliothéques, $14.00 
par an aux Etats-Unis et au Canada; $12.00 dans 
tous les autres pays. Le montant de l’abonnement 
pour étudiants diplémés (non-membres) recomman- 
dés par un professeur d’université ou d’une institu- 
tion analogue, $6.00 par an. Le montant de l’abon- 
nement doit étre versé au bureau du Secrétaire de 
la Société d’Econométrie, Box 1264, Yale Station, 
New Haven, Connecticut, U.S.A. Tout versement 
doit étre fait en monnaie américaine. 
Les manuscrits destinés a la publication doivent 
étre adressés—préférablement en trois copies et 
tapés a double interligne—a Robert H. Strotz, 
Managing Editor of Econometrica, Northwestern 
University, Evanston, Illinois, Etats-Unis, ou a M. 
Edmond Malinvaud, I.N.S.E.E., 29 Quai Branly, 
Paris, France.
